# The Case of the Borrowed Smiles

```meta
id: the-case-of-the-borrowed-smiles
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.8
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 5 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 5
words: 864
minutes: ~6
date: 2026-06-18
source_chat: Alex's truth-telling smile power
source_uuid: acdc6ac6-647b-4c05-a1a1-c470087d8248
prompt: Felix Amelia Alex, Alex (43 Evy mum), Alex (45 neighbour and father of Amelia).  new adventure, with powers, Alex (5) has a power where when he truly smiles at someone they have to
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: Four crisp affirmations. 'Catch smiles by giving them away.'
```

## Story

It started with a smile that wouldn't come.

Felix and Alex were eating toast in the kitchen when Daddy looked up from his tea and said, "Hm. That's odd." He was looking out the window at the fifteen-acre field. The grass was its normal green. The sky was its normal blue. But Mrs. Okafor from the post office was standing in the middle of the field, frowning at her own hands.

"Why does she look so cross?" asked Felix, who noticed loud things.

"She doesn't look cross," said Alex quietly. "She looks like she's *trying* to smile and can't find it."

Daddy made another cup of tea, because that is what Daddy does. "Better go and look, hadn't you," he said.

---

They ran across the field — Felix in front, Alex watching where the puddles were — and on the way they collected Amelia, who was already outside with her notebook.

"Three people in town can't smile," Amelia said, falling into step. "I wrote it down. Mr. Pemberton at the bakery. The lady at the fountain. And now Mrs. Okafor."

That's when the gold box appeared.

It dropped out of nowhere with a soft **BONG** and landed in the grass between them — bright gold, with a glowing question mark spinning on top. It only does that on important Saturdays.

Felix smacked it open. A warm light wrapped around Alex.

"What did you get?" said Felix, hopping.

Alex felt it before he understood it. "When I really smile at someone — properly, all the way — they have to tell me one true thing."

"That's a *quiet* power," said Felix, slightly disappointed there was nothing to punch.

"The quiet ones are usually the useful ones," said Amelia, writing it down.

---

They followed the missing smiles into the town square. And there, by the fountain, sat a small round man in a long grey coat, holding a jar. Inside the jar, hundreds of tiny golden smiles were swirling like fireflies.

"AHA," said Felix, pointing. "A smile thief!"

The man jumped. "I'm not a thief," he said. "I'm a *collector*. Bartholomew Quince. Collector of Lovely Things." He held the jar closer. "These are the loveliest things of all."

Alex stepped forward. He didn't shout. He just looked at Bartholomew very gently — and then he smiled. A real one. All the way to his eyes.

The power did its work.

Bartholomew's mouth wobbled. "I... haven't been smiled at in eleven years," he said, and the truth tumbled out of him. "Nobody smiles at me. So I thought — if I had a jar full of smiles, I could look at them whenever I liked. And not feel so alone."

The square went quiet. Felix's pointing finger came down.

"You don't catch smiles in a jar," said Felix simply. "You catch them by giving them away."

(Felix always says the important thing simply. That's who he is.)

Alex crouched beside the jar. "If you let them go, the people get them back. And then *you'll* have people who smile at you. Real ones. Better than jar ones."

Bartholomew looked at the swirling gold. He looked at Alex. He looked, for a long moment, at his own lonely reflection in the fountain.

Then he unscrewed the lid.

The smiles rushed out in a **WHOOOOSH** of gold, scattering across town — *zip* to Mrs. Okafor, *zip* to Mr. Pemberton, *zip* to the lady at the fountain — and everywhere they landed, faces lit up like windows at suppertime.

And Bartholomew, watching, smiled too. His very own. The first in eleven years.

"Oh," he said, surprised. "That *is* better."

---

They walked him to the Thursday Support Group, where Janet had made shortbread and Victor's Victoria sponge was, finally, excellent. Bartholomew sat down nervously. A fox by the snack drawer gave him a nod.

"You'll fit right in," said Amelia.

Back home, Daddy had the kettle on (of course). Mummy was singing in the kitchen. The field was green, the sky was blue, and the whole town, behind them, was full of smiles that belonged to the right people again.

"Good day's work," said Daddy, handing them each a biscuit.

Alex didn't say anything. He just smiled — the real all-the-way kind.

He didn't need a power for that one.

---

*P.S. Bartholomew kept exactly one smile, in a tiny jar, on his windowsill. It was his own. He gave it back to himself every morning when he woke up, just to remember he could.*

*P.P.S. Mrs. Okafor now smiles at everyone who comes into the post office. Especially the round man in the grey coat, who visits every Tuesday and never collects a single thing.*

---

**Felix and Alex's True Things to Remember:**

🌟 *A smile is the one thing that grows when you give it away.*

🌟 *When someone is unkind, they are often just lonely. Be brave enough to be kind first — like Alex.*

🌟 *The quiet things you notice can be just as powerful as the loud things you do.*

🌟 *You are worth smiling at, exactly as you are.*

---

Sleep tight, brave boys. 💛

*[END]*

# The Big Kids Club

```meta
id: the-big-kids-club
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.8
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 5 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 5
words: 1486
minutes: ~11
date: 2025-12-16
source_chat: Felix and Alex's adventure group
source_uuid: 8d93ba3d-2619-4e19-8e9a-8bace5d78b89
prompt: Tell me another story about the friends where Alex and Felix have turned 6, but Violet and Theo are still 5.
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: DISCOVERY. Inclusion/apology/not excluding friends. Different cast texture (Nana Rose) but top-tier values.
```

## Story

Felix and Alex had just turned six years old, and they both agreed that being six was much more important than being five. Six-year-olds, they had decided, were practically grown-ups compared to five-year-olds.

"We should start a Big Kids Club," Felix announced to Alex as they sat in their garden after their birthday party, still wearing their paper crowns and feeling very important indeed.

"What's a Big Kids Club?" Alex asked, though he thought it sounded like an excellent idea.

"It's for children who are six and older," Felix explained seriously. "We can have meetings and important discussions about big kid things."

Alex nodded wisely. "Like what?"

Felix thought hard. "Like... how to tie shoelaces properly. And reading chapter books. And..." He paused, trying to think of other grown-up six-year-old activities.

"And helping five-year-olds with difficult things," Alex added helpfully.

This seemed like a very mature and responsible thing for a Big Kids Club to do.

When Theo and Violet came over later that morning, the twins were sitting behind a cardboard sign that said "BIG KIDS CLUB - MEMBERS ONLY" in Alex's careful writing.

"What's that?" Violet asked, pointing at the sign.

"Our Big Kids Club," Felix said importantly. "It's only for children who are six and older."

Theo's face fell. "But we're still five."

"Exactly," Felix said, though he was starting to feel a bit mean about it. "You'll have to wait until you're six to join."

"That's not fair," Violet said, her bottom lip wobbling slightly. "We're your best friends."

"Being six doesn't make you better than us," Theo added, sounding hurt.

Alex looked at Felix uncertainly. The Big Kids Club had seemed like such a good idea five minutes ago, but now their friends looked sad and he felt horrible about it.

---

The twins spent the morning in their Big Kids Club, but it wasn't nearly as fun as they'd expected. Without Theo's stories and Violet's imagination, all they could think of to do was practice tying their shoes (which they could already do) and reading books (which was more fun with friends to talk to about the stories).

Meanwhile, Theo and Violet sat on Theo's front step, looking miserable.

"I don't want to wait until I'm six," Violet said sadly. "That's not for ages and ages."

"Maybe we should start our own club," Theo suggested, but he didn't sound very enthusiastic about it.

That's when Nana Rose appeared from her garden with a plate of freshly baked biscuits and the wise expression she got when she knew exactly what was wrong.

"You both look like you've lost a pound and found a penny," she said, settling down beside them on the step. "What's the matter?"

They explained about the Big Kids Club and how they weren't allowed to join because they were only five.

Nana Rose listened carefully, nodding at all the important parts. "I see," she said when they finished. "And are Felix and Alex having a wonderful time in their special club?"

"I don't know," Theo admitted. "They wouldn't let us see."

"Hmm," Nana Rose said thoughtfully. "When I was a little girl, I remember thinking that being older made me very important indeed. But do you know what I learned?"

"What?" Violet asked.

"That the best things in life are the ones you share with the people you care about, no matter how old they are. Age is just a number - it's kindness and friendship that really matter."

---

By lunchtime, Felix and Alex were thoroughly bored with their Big Kids Club. They'd read two books, practiced their writing, and tried to have an "important discussion" about what six-year-olds should eat for lunch. But it wasn't any fun without Theo making up funny voices for the characters and Violet drawing pictures to go with the stories.

"Maybe," Alex said quietly, "being six isn't as different from being five as we thought."

"Maybe," Felix agreed, "we were being a bit silly about the whole thing."

They were just wondering how to fix things when they heard crying coming from Violet's garden next door. Not the loud crying of someone who was hurt, but the quiet sniffling crying of someone who was very sad.

"That's Violet," Felix said, his stomach twisting with guilt.

They abandoned their Big Kids Club immediately and ran to the fence that separated their gardens.

Violet was sitting under her favorite apple tree, surrounded by art supplies, but instead of drawing her usual happy pictures, she was making a card covered in sad faces and grey scribbles.

"Violet?" Alex called softly. "Are you okay?"

She looked up with red eyes and a wobbly chin. "I was trying to make a special card to show you that five-year-olds can do big kid things too," she said. "But it just looks stupid."

Felix felt like he'd swallowed a stone. "It doesn't look stupid," he said, climbing over the fence to get a better look. "It looks sad, but that's because you're sad, isn't it?"

Violet nodded miserably.

Alex climbed over the fence too and sat down beside her. "We're sorry we made you sad," he said. "We didn't mean to."

"We were being silly about being six," Felix added. "Just because we're a bit older doesn't mean we're better than you."

"Really?" Violet asked hopefully.

"Really," both twins said together.

---

They found Theo in his garden, building what looked like a very elaborate fort out of cardboard boxes and blankets. But when they got closer, they realized it wasn't a fort at all - it was a clubhouse.

"FIVE-YEAR-OLDS RULE" was written in crayon on a piece of paper stuck to the front.

"Are you making your own club?" Alex asked.

Theo looked up from where he was trying to tape two boxes together. "Well, you have your Big Kids Club, so Violet and I need our own club for people who aren't six yet."

"But you're building it all by yourself," Felix pointed out. "Where's Violet?"

"She said she was too sad to help," Theo admitted. "And it's harder to build things when you're sad and there's only one of you."

Felix and Alex looked at each other. Their Big Kids Club had been lonely and boring, Violet was sad and making grey pictures, and Theo was building a clubhouse all by himself because they'd hurt their friends' feelings.

"This is all wrong," Felix said firmly.

"Very wrong," Alex agreed.

---

"We want to stop the Big Kids Club," Felix announced to Theo and Violet fifteen minutes later. "And we want to say sorry for being mean."

"You do?" Theo asked hopefully.

"Yes," Alex said. "Being six isn't actually that different from being five. We still like the same games and the same friends and the same stories."

"And clubs aren't fun if your best friends can't join them," Felix added.

Violet's face lit up like sunshine after rain. "So we can all play together again?"

"Yes," both twins said immediately.

"But," Alex added thoughtfully, "maybe we could still have a club. Just a different kind."

"What kind?" Theo asked, abandoning his half-built clubhouse completely.

"A Best Friends Club," Felix suggested. "For people who are good friends, no matter how old they are."

"That's a brilliant idea," Violet said, clapping her hands together.

"Much better than a Big Kids Club," Theo agreed.

---

They spent the rest of the afternoon building the Best Friends Club headquarters, which was a combination of Theo's cardboard boxes and blankets from all their houses. Unlike the exclusive Big Kids Club, the Best Friends Club was open to anyone who was kind and fun to play with.

They made membership cards (Violet drew them), wrote a club song (Theo composed it), and established the rules (Alex wrote them down very carefully). The most important rule was that no one could be left out just because of how old they were.

"This is much more fun than the Big Kids Club," Felix admitted as they sat in their new headquarters sharing biscuits that Nana Rose had contributed to the cause.

"That's because the best clubs are the ones where everyone feels welcome," Alex said wisely.

"And the best friends are the ones who stay friends even when you're being a bit silly," Violet added, grinning at the twins.

"I forgive you for being silly," Theo said generously, "because you fixed it and said sorry."

As they sat in their clubhouse, eating biscuits and planning all the adventures the Best Friends Club would have together, Felix and Alex realized something important: being six might mean they were a bit older, but it didn't mean they needed different friends or had to act too grown-up to have fun.

The very best thing about birthdays wasn't getting older - it was having friends who wanted to celebrate with you, no matter what age you were.

---

*The End*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# Winchester and the Laser Tag Mystery

```meta
id: winchester-and-the-laser-tag-mystery
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.8
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 4 / enjoy 5 / audio 5 / craft 5
words: 955
minutes: ~7
date: 2026-06-19
source_chat: Winchester the teddy bear and laser tag guns
source_uuid: 06095567-b992-4d58-9ffb-011245ae304d
prompt: new story, new character "Winchester" the teddy bear, he's visiting from school. Alex Felix Winchester, and the laser tag guns (boys got them for a birthday, like laser tag but onl
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: DISCOVERY. 'Asking is braver than force.' Sharing opens doors. Excellent.
```

## Story

It was a Friday, and Friday was a very important day. Friday was the day Winchester came home from school.

Winchester was the class teddy bear, soft and brown with one slightly wonky ear, and every weekend a different child got to take him home. This weekend it was Felix and Alex's turn, and they had been waiting all week.

"He's smaller than I remembered," said Felix, holding Winchester up to the light.

"He's exactly the same size," said Alex, who noticed these things. "You're just excited."

Felix was very excited. So was Alex, though Alex showed it by going quiet and careful, which was Alex's way.

That afternoon, the boys decided to show Winchester the best thing they owned: the laser tag guns they'd got for their birthday. The guns were brilliant. You could shoot another gun and it would lose a life, and you could switch the gun to different types — the Scatter (loud, close range, not very accurate), the Long Shot (quiet, far range, deadly precise), and the Bouncer (fired round corners, completely unpredictable).

"Winchester gets a gun too," Felix decided, and propped Winchester up against the sofa with the smallest gun balanced in his paws.

"He can't actually shoot," said Alex.

"He can supervise," said Felix.

And that was when the strangest thing happened.

The guns started glowing.

Not the normal glow. A soft, golden, *magic* glow, the kind that means something in the countryside is about to get wonderfully odd. And when the boys looked down, Winchester's button eyes had gone bright and twinkly, and his wonky ear had perked all the way up.

"Hello," said Winchester, in a small warm voice. "I've been waiting ages for someone to give me a gun."

Felix dropped his. Alex did not drop his, because Alex rarely dropped anything, but his eyes went very wide.

"You can *talk*," Felix whispered.

"Only on the weekends," said Winchester. "And only with children who share their toys. You shared. So here I am." He hopped down off the sofa and gave his arms a stretch. "Now. We have a problem. The Sock Goblin has been in your house."

"The *what*?" said Felix, delighted.

Winchester led them upstairs, gun bouncing on his little shoulder. He explained as they went. The Sock Goblin lived in the gap behind the radiator. He wasn't a bad goblin, exactly. He was a lonely one. He took single socks — never pairs, only singles — because he liked having something soft, and he was too shy to ever ask.

"That's why socks always go missing," said Alex slowly, putting it together. "It's not the washing machine."

"It was never the washing machine," said Winchester gravely.

They found the Sock Goblin behind the radiator in Felix and Alex's room. He was about the size of a teapot, grey and grumpy-looking, sitting on a nest of mismatched socks with his arms crossed.

"Go away," he muttered. "I'm not giving them back."

Felix raised his laser tag gun. The Scatter. Loud and dramatic.

But Alex put a hand on his arm. "Wait," he said quietly. "Look at him."

And Felix looked. The goblin wasn't fierce. He was hugging a stripy sock like it was the only friend he had. His grumpy face was really a sad face wearing a grumpy mask.

Felix lowered the gun. He thought about it, and then he said the thing simply, the way he always did when it mattered.

"You don't have to steal them," said Felix. "You could just *ask*. We'd share."

The goblin blinked. "Ask?"

"That's how Winchester got here," said Alex. "We shared. That's all it takes."

Winchester nodded wisely from the doorway.

So the goblin asked. Shyly, in a small voice, he asked if he could maybe keep just *three* socks — not to take, but to *borrow*, properly, with permission. And the boys said yes, of course, and Alex even found him a soft purple one from the odd-sock drawer that nobody had a match for anyway.

The goblin's grey face went pink with happiness. He'd never been *given* anything before. He'd only ever taken, because he didn't know he was allowed to ask.

They never did use the laser tag guns to shoot anyone. Winchester said afterward that the best agents almost never have to. *"The gun is for when you're brave,"* he said. *"But asking gently is braver. And kindness wins more battles than the Long Shot ever could."*

That night, Winchester sat between Felix and Alex on the bed, telling them about all the other weekends he'd had, all the other children, all the other quiet magic.

"Will you remember this on Monday?" Felix asked. "When you go back to school and you're just a normal bear again?"

"I'll remember," said Winchester. "Bears always remember the kind ones."

And the Sock Goblin, behind the radiator, hugged his three socks and his one purple one, and for the first time in a very long time, he wasn't lonely at all.

*The End.*

---

**The little affirmations woven through, for the boys to catch:**
- *Sharing opens doors* — Winchester only comes alive for children who share
- *Look before you act* — Alex stops Felix from shooting and sees the real story
- *Asking is brave* — the goblin's whole problem was he never learned he was allowed to ask
- *Kindness beats force* — they win without firing a shot

Want me to do another with Winchester before he goes back Monday — maybe one where the laser tag guns actually get used properly, against something that isn't sad, just silly? Or bring in Elfine and Marion for a weekend visit while he's here?

*[END]*


===STORY===

# Case File #31: The Scarecrow Who Forgot His Name

```meta
id: case-file-31-the-scarecrow-who-forgot-his-name
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.8
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 5 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 5
words: 1012
minutes: ~7
date: 2026-07-04
source_chat: Cross-story research adventure
source_uuid: 23ab97ed-9de2-4d19-8cfc-5f856841d995
prompt: can you show it in the chat? rather than a file
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: Loneliness-not-scariness. Naming as kindness. Read & confirmed.
```

## Story

It started with the animals.

Not one animal. All of them. The birds stopped landing in the fifteen-acre field. The rabbits wouldn't cross it. Even Willow the cat, who was afraid of absolutely nothing, sat at the very edge of the grass one evening, tail puffed up like a bottle brush, and refused to go one paw further.

"Something's wrong with the field," said Alex, and because Alex almost never said things unless they were true, Felix was already halfway to the shed.

HQ smelled like biscuits, which meant Amelia had got there first. She sat on her usual crate, notebook nowhere to be seen — she'd left it at home on purpose, she said, because "sometimes you just have to *look*."

"Boxes," said Felix, pointing.

Two gold question-mark boxes sat glowing on the shelf, right next to the growing stone.

**FELIX opened his first.** Out came a small warm light that settled behind his eyes and made everything inside him feel enormous. **Boing-Shout** — when Felix shouted at something scary, the scary thing would go *boing*, like a cartoon, and stop being frightening. Just for a second. Sometimes longer.

**ALEX opened his.** His power arrived quieter, the way his powers always did — a kind of stillness that dropped into his chest like a stone into a pond. **True-Sight.** When Alex looked hard at something, really looked, he could see what it actually was underneath — not what it seemed to be.

*Remember, boys — the bravest thing isn't never being scared. It's looking anyway.*

They went to the field.

---

The field looked ordinary. Long grass. A gate. And, standing right in the middle of it, one old scarecrow, arms out, sagging slightly to the left, the way scarecrows do when nobody's fixed them in a long time.

"That's just Bramble," said Amelia. "He's been there since before I was your age."

But when the wind moved through the grass, something moved *with* it — a shadow that slid off the scarecrow and rippled across the field like spilled ink, and every bird within a mile turned and flew the other way.

Felix, without waiting for anyone, shouted at it. **"BOO!"**

*BOING.*

The shadow bounced like a spring, shrank to the size of a football, and hovered there, wobbling, looking faintly embarrassed for a shadow.

"That's new," said Amelia.

But it didn't go away for good. It seeped back into the scarecrow, and the field went cold and grey again, and the birds stayed gone.

Alex hadn't said anything. He was looking at the scarecrow the way he looked at things when True-Sight was working — very still, very patient. Everyone else saw a scary shadow-throwing scarecrow.

Alex saw something else.

"He's not scary," Alex said quietly. "He's lonely. Look at his face."

---

They walked closer, slowly, the way you walk toward something you're not sure about yet — which, Amelia said, is its own kind of brave.

Up close, the scarecrow's painted-on smile had worn almost all the way off. His straw was thin and grey, not gold. And pinned to his chest, where a name tag should have been, there was only a small hole where a button used to be.

"He used to have a name," said Alex, crouching down. "Someone sewed it on. It's fallen off."

"Everyone forgot," said a voice — small, dry, rustling, like wind through old paper. It was the scarecrow. "Years and years. Nobody plants near me anymore. Nobody says my name. So I started making the shadow, just so *something* would notice I was here."

Felix's shout-power sat quiet in his chest. This wasn't a monster to boing away. This was somebody who needed something entirely different.

"What was your name?" Felix asked. Simply. The way Felix always asked the important things.

"I don't remember," the scarecrow whispered. "That's the worst part."

---

Alex found the button in the grass — half-buried, brass, dull with age — three feet from the scarecrow's left boot, exactly where True-Sight told him to look. He wiped it clean on his sleeve. There, scratched into the metal in old careful writing:

**BRAMBLE.**

"Your name is Bramble," Alex said, and held it up so the scarecrow could see it.

For a moment, nothing happened.

Then Felix — because somebody had to do the actual doing — climbed right up on Amelia's shoulders (she let him, just this once, just for this) and pinned the button back onto Bramble's chest, exactly where it had always belonged.

"There," Felix said. "Bramble. That's you. We remembered you."

The straw rustled. The grey began to lift, slowly, like fog burning off in the morning — the way sadness does, when someone finally says your name like they mean it. The shadow didn't come back. It didn't need to.

A single bird — brave, or maybe just curious — landed on Bramble's shoulder and stayed there.

"Nobody forgets you if we come and visit," said Alex.

"You could come with us sometimes," said Felix. "Not moving. Just — visiting. Everyone deserves someone remembering their name."

---

They visit Bramble every week now. Amelia brings the button back if it ever works loose, though it hasn't. The birds came back within two days. Willow still won't sit closer than the gate, but Willow has opinions about everyone and this is not new information.

Robert made tea when they got home — extra strong, the way he does when something has been quietly important — and listened to the whole story without once interrupting, which is its own kind of remembering-someone-matters.

---

*P.S. — Bramble has requested, politely, that his straw be re-stuffed before winter. Felix has volunteered. Alex has pointed out that Felix is not, in fact, qualified to re-stuff a scarecrow. Felix remains undeterred.*

*P.P.S. — It turns out being sad and lonely doesn't make you scary. It just makes you need someone to notice. That's true for scarecrows. It's true for everyone.*

**— End of Case File #31 —**

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Wrong Powers

```meta
id: the-wrong-powers
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.7
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 5 / enjoy 4 / audio 4 / craft 5
words: 1238
minutes: ~9
date: 2026-06-09
source_chat: Felix and friends with the wrong powers
source_uuid: 3dea258a-aeb1-49fa-8dee-bb736e0f1747
prompt: Felix Alex Theo and Violet - the wrong powers (like the wrong trousers Wallace and gromit) Keep it under 1500 words. Felix can fly (unspecified how)
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: Powers swap = empathy lesson. Automated under-scored affirm/emotion.
```

## Story

It started, as most problems do, with a gold box.

Theo found it on the doorstep of HQ at eight o'clock on a Saturday morning. He was already wearing his kit — backpack, notebook, the special pen with four colours — because Theo did not believe in being unprepared on Saturdays.

"Interesting," he said, which was what Theo said instead of *oh no*.

The box was different to the usual boxes. Instead of one question mark, it had four — and they were all wobbling.

By the time Felix and Alex arrived (Felix at a run, Alex already watching the box from six steps back), a girl was standing next to Theo. She had dark plaits, a yellow coat, and an expression like she was waiting to be impressed.

"This is Violet," said Theo. "She lives two fields over. She's seen us do things."

"What things?" said Felix.

"Flying," said Violet. "Twice. Also you went invisible on Tuesday but I wasn't sure if that counted."

"It counted," said Alex quietly.

Felix looked at the box. The question marks were definitely wobbling. One of them had gone sideways.

"Should we open it?" he said.

"Absolutely," said Felix.

"I was asking everyone," said Felix.

"I know," said Felix. He opened it.

---

Four smaller boxes tumbled out — gold, each with a single question mark, each with a name written on the lid in neat copper letters. *Felix. Alex. Theo. Violet.*

Felix opened his immediately.

A small glass marble rolled into his palm. It was very still. Very quiet. He looked at it. It looked at nothing.

"What does it do?" said Violet.

Felix concentrated hard, the way he always did with new powers, scrunching his nose up. Nothing happened. He scrunched harder. Still nothing. He stood very carefully and paid attention to everything around him — the creak of the shed, a bird in the oak, the smell of damp grass.

He noticed that Alex had a smudge of something on his left elbow.

He noticed that Theo's shoelace was coming undone.

He noticed that the wobbling on the empty box had completely stopped.

"Oh," said Felix, in a small and unfamiliar voice. "I think mine is quiet."

---

Alex opened his box.

A tiny pair of wings unfolded in his palm — bright red, smaller than his thumb, but already vibrating. He barely had time to look surprised before WHOOOOSH — he shot straight up into the air like a rocket, did a full loop over the HQ roof, came back down sideways, overcorrected, knocked over the watering can, and landed in a heap in the gooseberry bush.

"THAT'S MY POWER," said Felix, deeply offended.

"I know!" said Alex, upside down in the bush. He sounded delighted and also a bit dizzy.

Theo opened his box without ceremony. A small golden spanner appeared. He tried to pick it up. It vanished. He tried again. It vanished again. On the third try it bonked him firmly on the nose.

"BONG," said the spanner.

"Interesting," said Theo, which was now definitely *oh no*.

Violet looked at her box for a long moment.

"Mine's going to be the most sensible one," she announced, and opened it.

An enormous sandwich appeared in her hands.

It was very thick. There was cheese in it, and something orange, and possibly a small amount of glitter.

She looked at it.

The sandwich looked back.

"Is this a power?" she said.

"Eat it," said Felix immediately.

"That seems unscientific," said Theo, holding his nose.

Violet took a bite.

Her eyes went very wide. "I know where things are," she whispered. "Everything. The watering can — it's by the gooseberry bush. Your snack drawer — second shelf, behind the emergency biscuits. There's a—" she turned to face the field — "there's a hedgehog under the stone bridge right now and she has four hoglets and they're all called something beginning with B."

"Brenda," said Alex, from inside the gooseberry bush.

"Brenda," confirmed Violet. "And three others."

---

The problem, it turned out, was not that the powers were wrong. The problem was that someone had put them in the wrong boxes.

Alex worked this out first. He was hovering six feet off the ground, which was Felix's power, and Felix was sitting on an upturned bucket noticing things very quietly, which was Alex's power, and neither of them was doing their own job, and it was making the world slightly crooked.

"The boxes got mixed up," said Alex, from the air.

"Obviously," said Felix, from the bucket. He had just noticed that there was a small tear in the hem of Theo's left trouser leg and that the robin on the garden wall had been watching them for eleven minutes. Neither of these things were useful.

"Can we swap them?" said Violet.

"I don't think you can un-eat a sandwich," said Theo. He was now sitting at his desk writing a report about the spanner. The spanner had bonked him twice more and he was including diagrams.

Felix stood up. He looked at Alex in the air. Alex looked at Felix on the ground.

"Come down," said Felix. "Carefully."

Alex came down. Carefully. He only knocked over one thing (the evidence shelf sign, which fell without anything on the shelf moving, which Theo noted as significant).

Felix held his marble. Alex held his wings.

"What if you just did your job anyway," said Alex. "With the wrong power."

Felix thought about this. He was good at noticing things — he'd been noticing them for the last twenty minutes, he just hadn't been doing anything about them. He thought about Theo's shoelace. He thought about the smudge on Alex's elbow (marmalade, he was now certain). He thought about the wobbling box, which had stopped wobbling the moment he opened it.

He walked to the box. He picked it up. He turned it over.

Stuck to the bottom, in the same copper letters, was a tiny note:

*Sometimes the point isn't the power. It's learning what the other one does.*

Felix showed it to Alex.

Alex read it, and didn't say anything for a moment.

Then: "He noticed things. And I flew."

"Yes," said Felix.

"I think I understand you better now," said Alex.

Felix thought about being quiet for twenty minutes. The robin. The marmalade. The tear in Theo's trousers. Everything Alex saw every single day without saying it.

"I think I understand you better too," said Felix.

The wings in Alex's hand folded up. The marble in Felix's palm glowed once, warm and gold, and then went still.

From outside there was a sound: BONG.

"The spanner got me again," said Theo.

"Add it to the report," said Violet.

---

*That afternoon, Robert made tea and brought it to HQ on a tray, which he left outside the door without knocking, because he'd learned not to ask questions on Saturdays.*

*Violet ate the rest of the sandwich and located every missing sock in the county. She kept a list. It is six pages long.*

*The spanner is still in Theo's report. It has been added to the evidence shelf. It has bonked three more people. All of them said "interesting".*

*Alex went flying again that evening, on his own, with his own wings, because they were his wings after all. Felix watched from the garden.*

*He noticed Alex was grinning the whole time.*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Troll Who Forgot How To Laugh

```meta
id: the-troll-who-forgot-how-to-laugh
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.7
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 5 / enjoy 4 / audio 4 / craft 5
words: 3122
minutes: ~23
date: 2026-04-23
source_chat: Felix and Amelia save Halloween from the troll
source_uuid: 08e032e9-8dca-4263-a74a-02da5f77dad5
prompt: Q: Which returning villain would you like? A: Surprise us — pick the most interesting one
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: Model sad-before-bad. Automated under-scored audio (long free-verse tail); still excellent.
```

## Story

### *A Story for Felix and Alex*

---

It was Alex who noticed first.

This was usually how it went.

They were walking back from the forest — a regular Tuesday walk, nothing special, just the three of them and the field and the path through the long grass — when Alex stopped.

Felix walked two steps further and turned around.

"What?"

Alex was looking at the hedgerow along the lane. Specifically at a section of it that was grey.

Not autumn-grey. Not frost-grey. The deep, flat, unhappy grey of something that had lost whatever made it itself.

One section. About the width of a doorway. The leaves still there, still the right shape, just — drained.

"That wasn't grey yesterday," said Alex.

Felix looked at it.

Amelia came to look at it.

"No," said Amelia. "It wasn't."

Felix reached out and touched one of the grey leaves.

It didn't crumble. It didn't feel wrong exactly. It just felt like a leaf that had forgotten why it was a leaf.

"It's spreading from something," said Alex. He crouched down. Along the base of the hedge, barely visible in the grass — a trail. Not footprints exactly. More like a path of grey, as if something had walked this way and the grey had come off it onto everything it passed.

Six toes.

Felix looked at Alex.

Alex looked at Felix.

Amelia was already writing in her notebook.

*What has happened to the Halloween Troll?*

The answer came back slowly, which was unusual. The notebook normally answered quickly. This one arrived like it was being careful.

*The troll laughed for a week after Halloween. Then the laugh ran out. Trolls aren't built for sustained happiness — it moves through them like weather, and when it passes, the grey comes back worse than before. He's been in his hollow in the mossy part of the countryside for three months. The grey has been spreading outward from him in a slow circle. He doesn't know this. He isn't doing it on purpose. He's simply very sad, in the particular way of someone who was given a glimpse of something good and then watched it leave. The grey is grief. It just looks like the wrong colour.*

Amelia read it twice.

She showed it to Felix and Alex.

Felix read it.

He didn't say anything for a moment.

Then: "We should go."

"Yes," said Alex.

"Now."

"Yes."

Amelia looked at the grey trail leading away along the lane into the mossy countryside.

"It might be hard," she said. Not a warning. Just a fact.

"I know," said Felix. He looked at the grey leaf still between his fingers. "He laughed though. He properly laughed. That doesn't just disappear." He put the leaf down carefully. "It's still in there somewhere."

---

## The Boxes

They were on the five-bar gate at the edge of the mossy field.

Which was odd placing even by box standards — perched along the top bar in a row, slightly dewy, bobbing in a way that gate posts don't usually bob.

Felix hit his without breaking stride, jumping up to reach it.

*BONG.*

Warmth. Starting in his chest, spreading outward — his gold-power from Case #25, back again, the one that made things brave. But different this time. Fuller. Like the same instrument played by someone who had been practising.

He raised his hand toward the grey section of hedge.

The warmth-light touched it.

A single leaf — one — flickered back to green at the edge.

Just one. But real.

"Okay," said Felix. "That works. Slowly, but it works."

---

Alex hit his.

*BONG.*

He went very still — his stillness power, also returning, the deep clear-water stillness that let him know things.

He closed his eyes.

Opened them.

"He's close," said Alex. "Three fields over. In the hollow behind the big willow." He paused. "He knows we're coming."

"Is he—" Amelia started.

"Scared," said Alex. "Not of us. Of— himself. Of what he looks like now. Of us seeing it."

A pause.

"He's embarrassed," said Felix.

"Yes," said Alex. "Very."

Felix understood embarrassed. He understood it particularly well in the way that five-year-olds understand embarrassment — completely, physically, the full-body knowledge of it.

"Right," he said. "Then we don't make a big thing of it."

---

Amelia hit her box.

*BONG.*

Her power arrived as something she could only describe, writing it in her notebook later, as *reverse-reading.* She could look at something and understand its whole story — not just what it was now but how it had arrived at now. The path of it. Every hedge and gate and stone she looked at gave her a quiet history.

She looked at the grey trail.

She saw the troll walking it. Weeks ago at first — the grey just a shadow on things he touched. Then stronger. Then the circle spreading from his hollow outward as he sat and didn't move and the grey deepened.

She looked further along the trail.

She saw something else.

"The troll didn't lose the laugh," she said slowly.

Felix turned. "What?"

"The notebook said it ran out. Like weather." She looked at the trail. "But my power is showing me — something took it. Something came to the hollow about a month ago. Something small and quick and light-fingered." She looked up. "It didn't just fade. He was robbed."

Felix's eyes went wide.

Alex went still again.

"Who?" said Felix.

Amelia looked further along the trail with her story-reading eyes, following the thread of it.

"A Snip," she said.

"A what?" said Felix.

"A Snip. Small. Fast. Looks a bit like a pair of scissors if scissors could run." She looked up. "They collect laughter. Specifically recently-found laughter — the kind that belongs to someone who has never laughed before, or has laughed for the first time in a very long time. That kind is rare. That kind is valuable." She closed her notebook. "The troll's first laugh was exactly that kind. And a Snip came and took it while he was sleeping."

The field was quiet.

Felix looked at the grey trail.

"So he thinks the laugh left him," said Felix. "But it was stolen."

"Yes."

"And nobody told him."

"Nobody knew."

Felix stood up very straight.

"Right," he said. "Two things to do. Find the Snip. Then find the troll." He looked at Alex. "You track the Snip. Amelia reads the trail. I'll go to the troll."

"Alone?" said Amelia.

"Someone has to," said Felix simply.

Amelia looked at him.

Sometimes Felix was five years old and sometimes he was something older than that wearing a five-year-old, and the second thing was always slightly startling when it arrived.

"Right," she said. "Together as far as the willow. Then we split."

---

## The Snip

The Snip's trail was different from the troll's — quick and darting, here-then-there, the kind of path made by something that changed direction suddenly and often.

Alex tracked it with his stillness, standing at each point and knowing the next, until they found the Snip in the hollow of a fallen oak not far from the willow — a small, bright, metallic creature about the size of a large mouse, angular and quick, with two blades that weren't threatening exactly, more like very efficient-looking hands.

It was surrounded by small glowing things.

Laughs.

Dozens of them, each one slightly different — some big and round and golden, some thin and silver, some warm amber, some bright white. Stored in the hollow like a magpie's collection, each one with a faint sound that wasn't quite audible but which you could feel as a warmth at the back of the throat, the ghost of what they were.

The Snip saw them coming and went very still in the way of something deciding whether to run.

Alex sat down.

He sat on the ground in front of the hollow, cross-legged, no sudden movements, and looked at the Snip with his clear quiet attention.

The Snip looked back.

"We're not going to hurt you," said Alex.

The Snip made a sound — a quick metallic snick, like scissors opening and closing once.

"We need the troll's laugh back," said Alex. "The big golden one." He looked at the collection. "I think you know which one."

The Snip looked at its collection.

Alex waited.

The Snip moved toward a particular laugh — round, deep gold, still with the rumbling quality of boulders rolling downhill, the sound of a creature laughing for the first time in its whole life — and stopped.

"You kept it separate," said Alex. "From the others. You knew it was different."

The Snip made a smaller sound. Not snick. Something softer.

Amelia, behind Alex, was reading the Snip's story with her power, quietly, not interrupting.

"It's lonely," she said softly. "The laughs keep it company. It's not malicious. It just — collects warmth because it doesn't have its own."

Alex looked at the Snip.

"If you give the troll's laugh back," he said, "we'll come back and visit. Properly. That's a real offer."

The Snip looked at him for a long time.

Then it picked up the round golden laugh — delicately, with both blade-hands — and held it out.

Alex took it.

It felt exactly like what it was. Warm and round and rumbling and alive.

"Thank you," said Alex.

The Snip snicked once, quietly, and went back into its hollow.

Alex stood up and cupped the laugh in both hands.

"Go," said Amelia. "Felix needs us."

---

## The Troll

Felix had gone ahead alone.

The willow was enormous and old, its branches hanging down to the ground in long curtains, and behind those curtains a hollow — the troll's hollow, dark and mossy and quite large because the troll was quite large, with a smell of earth and damp and the deep nothing-smell of grey things.

Felix pushed through the willow branches.

The troll was there.

Much greyer than Felix remembered. Not just colour — shape. He seemed smaller, somehow, even though he was clearly the same size. Compressed. Folded in on himself, sitting against the back of the hollow with his six-toed feet in front of him and his large warty hands in his lap and his raisin eyes looking at exactly nothing.

He looked up when Felix came in.

And immediately looked away.

"Go away," said the troll.

His voice was different. The deep grumbly rumble was still there but it was flat. The way a bell sounds flat when you muffle it with your hand.

"No," said Felix pleasantly, and sat down on a root.

"I said—"

"I heard you," said Felix. "I'm sitting down anyway."

The troll said nothing.

Felix looked around the hollow. Grey moss. Grey roots. A small pile of things — pebbles, a stick, a piece of bark — that might have been sorted and arranged at some point and were now just there, untouched.

"I'm grey," said the troll. Not complainingly. Just stating it.

"I can see that," said Felix.

"It's spreading."

"I know."

"I'm not doing it on purpose."

"I know that too," said Felix.

The troll looked at his hands. Large, greenish-grey, warty. "The laugh," he said. Very quietly. "After Halloween. It was — for a week I kept — I couldn't stop—" He stopped. "And then it just went. One morning it wasn't there anymore and I thought at first it would come back but it didn't and—" He looked at his hands. "I think I'm just not something that gets to keep that."

Felix looked at him.

At this large grey sad creature who had danced his small smug dance on Halloween night and howled with laughter at his own happiness and eaten one toffee with enormous dignity.

"Can I tell you something?" said Felix.

The troll said nothing, which Felix took as yes.

"It didn't leave," said Felix. "It was taken. Something took it while you were asleep. Something called a Snip that collects first-laughs because they're rare and valuable." He watched the troll's face. "You didn't lose it. You're not someone who can't keep it. It was stolen."

The troll was very still.

"That's not—" he started.

"True?" said Felix. "My friend Alex has it right now. He's coming."

The troll looked at the curtain of willow branches.

Felix's warmth-power was spreading gently through the hollow — he wasn't pushing it, just letting it exist, the way you let a fire warm a room without pointing at anything specific. The grey at the edge of the hollow shifted slightly. Just at the edges.

"The grey," said the troll. "Did it—"

"The field hedge," said Felix. "A bit."

The troll closed his eyes. "I'm sorry."

"It's fixable," said Felix. "That's why we're here."

---

Alex came through the willow branches.

He stopped when he saw the troll. The troll opened his eyes and saw what Alex was holding.

The golden laugh sat in Alex's cupped hands, warm and round, catching no light because it made its own.

The troll looked at it.

His raisin eyes — which had been flat and dull — went different. Not happy yet. Something before happy. Something like recognition.

"That's—" he said.

"Yes," said Alex.

Alex walked across the hollow and held his hands out.

The troll looked at the laugh for a long time.

Then he reached out — carefully, more carefully than you'd expect from hands that size — and took it.

The moment it touched him the laugh didn't go back in gradually. It wasn't a slow process. It went back the way it had been taken — all at once, completely, every part of it, the whole first magnificent ridiculous joyful surprised laugh of the Halloween Troll's life, back where it belonged.

The hollow went gold for a moment.

Just a flash.

Then the troll laughed.

---

It was the same laugh.

Exactly the same. *HUH. HUH HUH. HEEHEEHEE. BWAHAHAHA.* The boulders-downhill laugh, the laugh that didn't know how to be small, filling the hollow and pushing out through the willow branches and sending three wood pigeons out of the tree above in a great startled clatter.

Felix started laughing too.

He couldn't help it.

Alex smiled — the quiet full smile he kept for the best moments.

Amelia appeared through the willow curtain, heard the laugh, and her face did the thing it rarely did, which was let everything she was feeling show at once.

The troll laughed until he ran out of breath, which took a while, and then breathed, and laughed again, and then sat back against the hollow wall and wiped his eyes.

The grey was going.

Not all at once, but unmistakably — the colour creeping back into the moss, the roots, the earth of the hollow. The troll's own greenish colour deepening, the flat drained quality lifting, his raisin eyes going bright.

"Oh," said the troll. He said it very simply. Like coming home.

"Better?" said Felix.

"Much," said the troll.

He looked at the three of them — Amelia at the entrance, Alex standing quietly, Felix on his root, the warmth-power still gently present.

"You came," said the troll.

"Obviously," said Felix.

"I thought—" the troll stopped. Then: "Nobody's come to the hollow before. In—" he thought about it— "a very long time."

"We'll come back," said Alex. "We told the Snip the same thing."

The troll looked at him. "You made a deal with the Snip?"

"It's lonely," said Alex. "Like you."

The troll was quiet.

"There's a support group," said Felix. Because of course he said it. He said it to everyone.

"You've mentioned this before," said the troll. "On Halloween. You said it then too."

"You didn't come," said Felix.

"No," said the troll.

"Thursday evenings," said Felix. "Victor makes a very good sponge now. Voldemort comes. He sits at the back. He ate two pieces last week."

The troll looked at Felix with his bright raisin eyes. "Voldemort."

"He's working on things," said Felix. "Like everyone."

The troll looked at his hands. Green again, properly green, the grey almost fully gone. He turned them over. Looked at his six toes.

"Thursday," he said.

"Thursday," said Felix.

---

## Going Home

The grey in the hedgerow was green by the time they walked back.

Not all of it immediately — it spread back the way the grey had spread, slowly, from the hollow outward, the colour following the laugh home. But the section they'd first noticed — the doorway-width of drained leaves on the lane — was already green and rustling and entirely itself by the time they passed it.

Felix touched one leaf as he walked past.

Just a leaf. Just green. Just a leaf being a leaf without any trouble.

He smiled.

"Good day," said Alex, beside him.

"Good day," agreed Felix.

Amelia was writing in her notebook — the Snip's hollow location, a note to revisit, thoughts on first-laughs and why they were rare and what it meant that the troll's had been so easy to steal (not because the troll was weak, she was writing, but because it had been so new, so unguarded, held the way you hold something you've just been given before you know it's yours to keep).

Felix looked over his shoulder once at the mossy countryside.

Far away, barely audible, carried on the wind like weather:

*HUH HUH HEEHEEHEE BWAHAHAHA.*

The troll, laughing to himself.

Just because.

Just because it was still there.

Felix turned back to the lane and kept walking.

Some things you just know are going to be okay.

---

🧌 **THE END** 🧌

---

*The troll came to the Thursday group.*
*First Thursday: he sat outside.*
*Second Thursday: he came in.*
*Third Thursday: he ate a piece of Victor's sponge.*
*He said it was adequate.*
*Victor has three Post-it notes now.*
*The third one — from the troll — is in the middle.*
*He says this is because it arrived last.*
*This is not why.*

*The Snip was visited the following Tuesday.*
*Alex brought it a laugh — not stolen, a genuine one, his own, cupped in his hands and offered.*
*The Snip held it for a moment.*
*Then gave it back.*
*Then made a sound that had never been recorded before.*
*Amelia wrote it down as best she could.*
*It was, she wrote, like scissors learning to be bells.*

*The grey section of hedgerow is the greenest part of the lane now.*
*Greener than the rest.*
*As if it's making up for lost time.*
*It probably is.*

*Case closed: home before tea.*
*Good crisps.*
*The right juice.*
*Completely fine.*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# Felix, Alex and the Tiny Sailor of Eastbourne

```meta
id: felix-alex-and-the-tiny-sailor-of-eastbourne
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.65
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 5 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 4
words: 1184
minutes: ~9
date: 2026-07-05
source_chat: Alex and Felix's Eastbourne beach adventure
source_uuid: b5df04bd-d327-4db4-9231-c68316779d76
prompt: please tell me a story with Alex Felix mummy daddy and an adventure at the beach in Eastbourne. try and work in a sail boat somehow.   Keep it under 1500 words
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: Self-acceptance: 'use who you are.' Real place.
```

## Story

Felix woke up already talking. "BEACH DAY BEACH DAY BEACH DAY!"

Alex just smiled at the ceiling. He'd known it was beach day since yesterday, when Daddy left the buckets by the front door without saying anything.

The drive to Eastbourne took ages and no time at all, the way beach drives always do. Mummy sang along to the radio. Daddy had packed the good thermos, the one that meant tea-in-a-crisis, tea-for-no-reason, and everything in between.

When they finally tumbled out of the car, the sea was enormous and silver and roaring gently, like it was clearing its throat before saying something important.

"Race you!" shouted Felix, already sprinting.

Alex walked. He liked to look first.

---

They built the usual things — a lopsided castle, a moat that the sea kept stealing sips from. Felix insisted on a flag made of seaweed. Mummy sat with her knees up, watching the waves with the kind of calm that made villains nervous, if there had been any villains, which today there weren't. Daddy poured tea into the little cups and didn't rush anyone.

It was Alex who noticed it.

Half-buried near a clump of rocks, something small and wooden was rocking gently in a rock pool, going nowhere. Not a shell. Not driftwood exactly.

"Felix," Alex said. Just his name. That was usually enough.

Felix was already there, on his knees, peering in.

It was a boat. A tiny one — no bigger than Daddy's hand — with a cracked mast and a sail made of something that had once been white and was now the colour of old teabags. And sitting in it, gripping the broken mast like it was the last solid thing in the world, was a sailor no taller than Felix's thumb.

He had a proper little coat, a proper little beard, and a face doing its absolute best not to cry.

"Hello," said Alex quietly, which is how Alex said most things.

"You're rather small," said Felix, which is how Felix said most things.

The sailor sniffed. "I'm CAPTAIN small, if you don't mind. Captain Bartholomew Bell. Or I was. Storm took my ship apart three tides ago. Been sitting in this puddle ever since, going nowhere, being nobody."

"You're not nobody," said Alex, and he said it the way he said things that mattered — once, and clearly.

Captain Bell looked up at him properly for the first time.

"Everyone who sails past is big," he said. "Big boats, big waves, big everything. I got so small and so lost I stopped thinking anyone would ever notice a person my size."

Felix sat down right next to the rock pool, getting his shorts wet and not caring one bit. "We noticed. Alex ALWAYS notices things first. It's basically his superpower."

"It's not a superpower," Alex mumbled, going a bit pink.

"It absolutely is," said Felix, "and mine is doing something about it. So. What do we do?"

---

Captain Bell explained, in a small crackly voice, that a sailor without a ship is just a very damp man with excellent knots and nowhere to use them. His mast was snapped. His sail was ruined. And worst of all, he said, he wasn't sure he even deserved a new one — after all, he'd let the last one get away from him.

"That's daft," said Felix immediately. "The storm did that, not you. You didn't do anything wrong. Sometimes big things happen and it's nobody's fault."

Alex nodded slowly. "You don't have to be big to matter. You just have to be *you*."

Mummy, watching from her spot on the sand, caught Daddy's eye and said nothing, which was exactly the right amount of saying something.

"Right," said Felix, standing up and clapping sand off his knees. "We need: a new mast. A new sail. And someone very good at knots, which — hello — we've got."

They gathered supplies like the most serious expedition in history. A lolly stick from Alex's pocket (he always kept useful things in his pockets) became the mast. A triangle torn carefully from Daddy's spare hanky became the sail — Daddy insisted, said it was his favourite hanky's finest hour. Two blue mussel shells, cleaned in a rock pool, became the hull, tied together with a bit of string Mummy just happened to have, because Mummy always just happened to have things.

Captain Bell directed the whole operation from Felix's palm, shouting things like "TIGHTER ON THE PORT KNOT" and "STEADY THE RIGGING" in a voice that got braver with every instruction.

When it was done, it wasn't a big boat. It was a very small, very wonky, very determined little boat.

"She'll do," said Captain Bell, and for the first time, he wasn't crying. "She'll absolutely do."

---

They carried the tiny ship down to where the waves came in soft and shallow, right at the edge, where the sea whispered instead of roared.

"Are you scared?" Alex asked the Captain.

"Terrified," admitted Captain Bell. "But being scared and being brave aren't opposites. You can be both at the same time. I only just remembered that today."

Felix grinned. "That's basically the best thing anyone's ever said."

They set the little boat down together, both boys' hands cupped around it like it was the most precious thing on the beach — which, for that moment, it was. A gentle wave lifted it, turned it, and the wonky mussel-shell hull with its hanky sail caught the wind and went.

Captain Bell stood at the tiny wheel, hat off, waving.

"THANK YOU, TRIO— sorry, wrong story," he called out. "THANK YOU, FELIX AND ALEX!"

"BON VOYAGE!" bellowed Felix, loud enough for the whole beach to hear.

Alex just watched, all the way until the little boat was a speck, then nothing at all. He didn't say anything. He didn't need to.

---

Daddy handed round the last of the tea, gone slightly cool, which somehow made it taste even better.

"You know," said Mummy, pulling Alex in for a squeeze and reaching for Felix's sandy hand at the same time, "it doesn't matter if you're big and loud like Felix, or quiet and noticing like Alex. Both ways of being in the world matter enormously. You don't have to change who you are to be brave. You just have to *use* who you are."

"That's what we did," said Felix, matter-of-factly, as if this had been obvious the whole time.

Alex smiled at the sea. Somewhere out there, a very small, very brave captain was sailing on, in a boat made of a lolly stick and a hanky and two mussel shells, because two five-year-old boys had decided that small didn't mean forgotten.

They walked back up the beach as the sun started to go orange, Daddy carrying the empty thermos, Mummy carrying two pairs of sandy shoes, and both boys carrying, without knowing it exactly, the same quiet feeling: that noticing mattered, that doing mattered, and that everybody — however small — deserves a sail.

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Big School Adventure

```meta
id: the-big-school-adventure
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.6
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 5 / enjoy 3 / audio 5 / craft 5
words: 1307
minutes: ~10
date: 2025-12-16
source_chat: Felix and Alex's adventure group
source_uuid: 8d93ba3d-2619-4e19-8e9a-8bace5d78b89
prompt: Tell me another story about Felix and Alex. When they are moving into year 1 at school.
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: DISCOVERY. Starting-school nerves handled beautifully. Directly useful for the boys' real life.
```

## Story

Felix and Alex were both five and three-quarters years old, and in exactly one week they would be starting Year One at the big school. This was very exciting and very scary all at the same time.

"Year One children have to sit at proper desks," Felix said, practicing sitting very straight on his bedroom chair. "And they have to learn proper writing, not just drawing pictures."

"And they have homework," Alex added nervously. "Real homework that you take home in a special book."

They had visited their new classroom the week before, and it looked enormous compared to their cozy nursery. The teacher, Mrs. Chen, seemed very nice, but she also seemed very serious and grown-up.

"What if we can't do the work?" Alex worried. "What if it's too hard?"

"What if we don't make any friends?" Felix added. "What if everyone else is cleverer than us?"

Their tummies felt wobbly just thinking about it.

The night before their first day, Mum came to tuck them into bed and found them both staring at the ceiling with very worried expressions.

"Nervous about tomorrow?" she asked gently, sitting down on Alex's bed.

"A little bit," Alex admitted.

"A lot," Felix said honestly.

Mum smiled. "Would you like to know a secret? When I started my first proper job, I was so nervous that I put my jumper on backwards and didn't notice until lunchtime."

The twins giggled despite their worries.

"Really?" Felix asked.

"Really. Being nervous about new things is perfectly normal. It just means you care about doing well."

"But what if we're not ready?" Alex said in a small voice.

"Then you'll learn as you go," Mum said firmly. "That's what school is for - learning new things bit by bit. You don't have to know everything on the first day."

---

The next morning, Felix woke up with butterflies dancing in his stomach. Alex was already awake, sitting on the edge of his bed looking pale and worried.

"My tummy feels funny," Alex said.

"Mine too," Felix agreed. "Like it's full of jumping beans."

They got dressed very carefully in their smart new school uniforms - grey shorts, white polo shirts, and red jumpers with the school logo. Alex's hair stuck up at the back no matter how much Mum combed it, and Felix managed to get toothpaste on his clean jumper before they'd even left the house.

"You both look very smart," Dad said, taking what felt like a hundred photographs. "Very grown-up indeed."

The walk to school seemed both too long and too short at the same time. Other children were walking the same way, some looking confident and chatty, others clinging to their parents' hands just like Felix and Alex were doing.

"Look," Alex whispered, pointing to a girl about their age who was crying quietly as her mum led her through the school gates. "She's scared too."

"And that boy's walking really slowly," Felix observed, watching a child who was clearly trying to delay reaching the classroom as long as possible.

Somehow, knowing they weren't the only nervous ones made them feel a tiny bit better.

---

Mrs. Chen was waiting in the classroom doorway with a big smile and a clipboard full of names.

"Felix and Alex!" she said warmly. "How lovely to see you again. Are you ready for your big school adventure?"

The classroom looked even more enormous than Felix remembered, with proper desks arranged in neat rows and educational posters covering every wall. There were children everywhere - some sitting quietly, some chattering excitedly, and some looking just as worried as the twins felt.

"Your table is over there," Mrs. Chen said, pointing to a group of four desks pushed together. "You'll be sitting with Sophie and James."

Sophie turned out to be the girl they'd seen crying at the gate, though she'd stopped crying now and was carefully arranging her pencils in a perfect row. James was a boy with freckles and gaps where his front teeth used to be.

"I lost my teeth last week," James announced proudly. "The tooth fairy gave me a pound coin."

"A whole pound?" Felix said, impressed.

"Each tooth," James confirmed. "I'm hoping more fall out soon."

Sophie looked up from her pencils. "I was scared about coming to school today," she said quietly. "But my mummy said everyone feels scared sometimes, and it's okay."

"We were scared too," Alex admitted.

"Really?" Sophie looked relieved. "You seemed so brave when you walked in."

"We weren't brave," Felix said. "We were just pretending to be brave until we felt brave."

"That's the same thing, isn't it?" James asked, lisping slightly through his tooth gaps.

---

The first activity was writing their names on special name tags. Felix had been practicing his letters all summer, but when he tried to write "FELIX" on the card, his hand felt shaky and the letters came out wonky.

"It doesn't matter if it's not perfect," Mrs. Chen said kindly, stopping by his desk. "The most important thing is that you tried your best."

She showed him how to hold his pencil properly, and the second time his name looked much better.

Alex's name was longer and more difficult, but Sophie helped him sound out the letters, and James shared his rubber when Alex made a mistake.

"This is actually quite fun," Alex whispered to Felix.

"And nobody's work is perfect," Felix whispered back, looking around at all the other wonky name tags.

The morning continued with story time (sitting on the carpet like they used to do in nursery), counting games with colorful blocks, and a tour of the school to find important places like the toilets and the library.

By playtime, the four table-partners had discovered they all liked the same playground games and had the same worries about homework.

"My big sister says Year One homework is easy," Sophie confided as they played on the swings. "She says it's just practicing things you already know how to do."

"And my brother says the teachers help you if you get stuck," James added. "He says they don't expect you to know everything straight away."

---

After lunch, they did their first proper lesson - learning about the sounds that letters make. Felix and Alex found they knew more than they thought they did, and when they got stuck, Mrs. Chen helped them work it out.

"You're all doing brilliantly," she told the class as they packed up their things at the end of the day. "I can see you're going to have a wonderful year learning together."

As they walked home with Mum and Dad, Felix realized the butterflies in his stomach had stopped dancing.

"How was it?" Mum asked.

"Better than I thought," Alex said, sounding surprised.

"Much better," Felix agreed. "Mrs. Chen is really nice, and our table friends are funny, and the work isn't as hard as I thought it would be."

"And nobody knew everything already," Alex added. "We're all learning together."

That evening, as they got ready for bed, Alex said thoughtfully, "I think I was worried about the wrong things."

"What do you mean?" Felix asked.

"I was worried about not being clever enough or not knowing enough. But school isn't about already knowing things - it's about learning new things."

"And everyone's learning," Felix realized. "Even the children who seem really confident were probably nervous inside, like we were."

As they settled into bed, both twins felt proud of themselves for being brave (even when they didn't feel brave), for making new friends, and for discovering that starting Year One wasn't nearly as scary as they'd imagined.

"Tomorrow will be even better," Alex said sleepily.

"Because we'll know what to expect," Felix agreed.

And they were both right.

---

*The End*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Mixed-Up Birthday Magic

```meta
id: the-mixed-up-birthday-magic
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.6
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 4 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 5
words: 1394
minutes: ~10
date: 2025-12-16
source_chat: Felix and Alex's adventure group
source_uuid: 8d93ba3d-2619-4e19-8e9a-8bace5d78b89
prompt: And it's a story where Felix and Alex turned 100, but Theo and Violet got younger and turned 2.
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: DISCOVERY. Birthday-magic mix-up; sharing/gratitude.
```

## Story

Felix and Alex woke up on Wednesday morning feeling very strange indeed. When Felix tried to get out of bed, his knees creaked like old doors, and when Alex looked in the mirror, he saw a face covered in wrinkles with a long white beard.

"Alex!" Felix called in a voice that sounded like their great-grandfather's. "Something very odd has happened to us!"

"I know!" Alex replied, hobbling over with a walking stick that had appeared overnight. "We look like we're one hundred years old!"

They were both wearing old-fashioned clothes that were much too big for them - long coats and funny hats that kept falling over their eyes. When they tried to run downstairs like they usually did, they had to go very slowly, holding onto the banister with shaky hands.

"Boys?" called Mum from the kitchen. "Why are you walking so slowly? And why are your voices so different?"

When Mum saw them, she dropped her coffee cup in shock. "Felix? Alex? What on earth has happened to you?"

"We don't know," Felix said in his creaky old voice. "We just woke up like this."

Before Mum could answer, there was a loud crying sound from next door - but not normal crying. This sounded like tiny babies crying.

---

They found Theo and Violet sitting on Theo's front lawn, but they were the smallest toddlers Felix and Alex had ever seen. They couldn't have been more than two years old, with chubby cheeks and tiny hands, wearing clothes that were far too big for their little bodies.

"Waaaah!" cried tiny Theo, trying to walk but falling down onto his bottom. "Want my gran!"

"Mine mine mine!" babbled little Violet, clutching a crayon that was almost as big as she was.

"Oh my goodness!" said Theo's gran, who had come running when she heard the crying. "These children... they look like Theo and Violet, but they're just babies!"

Felix tried to kneel down to talk to their friends, but his old knees wouldn't let him get that low. "Theo! Violet! It's us - Felix and Alex!"

But tiny Theo just looked confused and reached up with his little arms, making "up up up" sounds like toddlers do when they want to be picked up.

Little Violet pointed at Felix's white beard and giggled. "Funny man!"

"They don't remember us," Alex said sadly. "They're too little."

---

Nana Rose appeared with a very thoughtful expression and two bottles of warm milk. "Well, well," she said, looking at the hundred-year-old twins and the two-year-old toddlers. "This is quite a pickle, isn't it?"

"Do you know what happened to us?" Felix asked hopefully. Nana Rose always seemed to understand mysterious things.

"I suspect," she said, settling down carefully on the grass, "that someone's been playing with birthday magic. Yesterday was quite ordinary, wasn't it? But sometimes, on very special days, magic gets a bit mixed up."

"Birthday magic?" Alex repeated.

"Oh yes. Magic that's supposed to make you grow up just the right amount, but sometimes it gets confused and gives some people too many years and others not enough."

Tiny Theo had toddled over to Nana Rose and was tugging on her skirt with his little fists. "Up!" he demanded.

"The question is," Nana Rose continued, lifting Theo onto her lap, "how do we fix it?"

"How do we fix anything when we're too old to move properly and our friends are too young to understand us?" Felix asked, feeling very sorry for himself.

"Perhaps," Nana Rose said with a twinkle in her eye, "that's exactly what you need to figure out."

---

Looking after two-year-old Theo and Violet turned out to be much harder than Felix and Alex had expected, especially when they were moving like hundred-year-olds.

Every time they tried to explain something, tiny Theo would get distracted by a butterfly or a fallen leaf. Little Violet kept trying to eat things that weren't food - flowers, crayons, and even Alex's walking stick when he wasn't looking.

"This is impossible," Alex groaned, lowering himself carefully onto a park bench while he watched tiny Theo chase a pigeon in very wobbly circles.

"Maybe that's the point," Felix said thoughtfully. "Maybe the birthday magic got mixed up because we needed to learn something."

"Learn what?"

Felix watched as little Violet sat down heavily and started to cry because she couldn't reach a pretty flower that was just a bit too far away. Without thinking, he hobbled over and picked the flower for her. Her whole face lit up with joy.

"Maybe," Felix said slowly, "we need to learn what it's like to take care of people, and what it's like to need taking care of."

---

As the day went on, something wonderful started to happen. Even though Felix and Alex were moving slowly and feeling tired like very old people, they discovered that they were actually quite good at understanding what tiny Theo and Violet needed.

When little Theo got frustrated because he couldn't build with his blocks properly, Alex sat down carefully and helped him stack them, using his slow, careful hands to show Theo how to balance them.

When tiny Violet got upset because her drawing didn't look the way she wanted it to, Felix told her gentle stories in his creaky voice until she stopped crying and started giggling instead.

And when both toddlers got tired and grumpy, the hundred-year-old twins discovered that they had endless patience for rocking them gently and singing quiet songs.

"You know," Alex said as he carefully carried a sleepy tiny Theo, "taking care of people feels rather nice."

"Even when it's difficult," Felix agreed, as little Violet held onto his finger with her tiny hand.

---

As the sun began to set, they all sat together in Theo's garden. The hundred-year-old twins were exhausted from their day of careful toddler-watching, and tiny Theo and Violet were yawning and rubbing their eyes.

"I think," said Nana Rose, who had been watching them all day with a very knowing smile, "that you've all learned something important today."

"What's that?" Felix asked, though he was beginning to suspect.

"That taking care of others and letting others take care of you are both very important things. And that kindness and patience matter more than how old you are."

As she spoke, something magical began to happen. Felix felt his back straightening, and his white beard began to shrink. Alex's wrinkles started smoothing out, and his voice began to sound younger.

At the same time, tiny Theo and Violet began to grow. Their chubby baby faces lengthened, their little bodies got bigger, and their baby babble turned back into proper words.

"Felix! Alex!" Theo said in his normal five-year-old voice, looking around in confusion. "What happened? I feel like I had the strangest dream about being very, very small."

"And I dreamed I was old and had a long beard," Felix said, touching his chin where whiskers had been just moments before.

"I think," Violet said thoughtfully, "that we all had the same dream. But it felt very real."

---

When they were all back to their proper ages - Felix and Alex six, and Theo and Violet five - they sat in the garden talking about their mysterious day.

"I liked taking care of you when you were tiny," Alex admitted. "Even though it was hard work."

"And I liked it when you took care of me," Violet said. "It felt safe and nice."

"Maybe that's what friends do," Theo said wisely. "Sometimes you take care of people, and sometimes people take care of you."

"And it doesn't matter how old anyone is," Felix added. "What matters is being kind and patient and helping each other."

Nana Rose brought them all cups of hot chocolate and smiled. "I think the birthday magic worked exactly as it was supposed to," she said. "Sometimes the most important lessons come in the most unexpected packages."

As they sipped their hot chocolate and watched the stars come out, they all agreed that it had been the strangest and most wonderful day any of them could remember. And if the birthday magic had been a dream, well, it was the kind of dream that taught them something important about friendship and caring for others.

---

*The End*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Wishing Tree Express

```meta
id: the-wishing-tree-express
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.6
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 4 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 5
words: 1956
minutes: ~14
date: 2025-12-16
source_chat: Felix and Alex's adventure group
source_uuid: 8d93ba3d-2619-4e19-8e9a-8bace5d78b89
prompt: Can you tell me another similar story, but a bit more whimsical please?
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: DISCOVERY. Strong warmth. Needs a confirming read for canon-fit.
```

## Story

The morning after the big thunderstorm, everything in the village looked different. Not broken-different, but shimmery-different, as if the rain had washed away an invisible layer of dust and revealed secrets that had been hiding underneath.

Violet noticed it first when she went to feed the birds in her garden. The old oak tree at the bottom looked exactly the same as always—enormous and ancient, with branches that spread like arms reaching for the sky. But something was definitely different about the way the light fell through its leaves, creating patterns that seemed to spell out words if you looked at them just right.

She was still trying to read the leaf-shadows when Felix appeared at the garden fence, practically bouncing with excitement.

"Violet! Come and see what Alex found!"

She followed him three gardens down to where Alex was standing beneath the twins' apple tree, staring up at something with the intense concentration he usually saved for particularly difficult math problems.

"What is it?" Violet asked.

Alex pointed up into the branches. "That."

Hanging from one of the lower branches was something that definitely hadn't been there yesterday: a small wooden sign, painted bright green with silver letters that caught the morning sunlight.

"WISHING TREE EXPRESS," Violet read aloud. "DEPARTURES DAILY WHEN WEATHER PERMITS."

"It wasn't here yesterday," Alex said with absolute certainty. "I climbed this tree yesterday afternoon, and there was no sign."

"Maybe the storm blew it here from somewhere else," Violet suggested, though she didn't sound entirely convinced.

"From where?" Felix asked. "And how would it hang itself up so perfectly?"

Before anyone could answer, Theo came running around the corner of the house, his face bright with excitement and something that might have been relief.

"There you are! I've been looking everywhere. Come and see—something amazing has happened!"

---

Theo led them through the village toward the old railway cutting, chattering the whole way about mysterious happenings and impossible things. The cutting had been empty for decades—the railway line had been removed long before any of them were born, leaving just a grassy path that people used for dog walking and nature walks.
