# The Library Keeper's Lost Words

```meta
id: the-library-keepers-lost-words
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.47
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 4.35 / enjoy 3.2 / audio 5.0 / craft 4.73
words: 1489
minutes: ~11
date: 2026-06-19
source_chat: Winchester the teddy bear and laser tag guns
source_uuid: 06095567-b992-4d58-9ffb-011245ae304d
prompt: another story please, include Alex Felix Winchester, big ted (massive teddy), Amelia, and the library keeper.   under 1500 words
reviewed: auto-scored (confirm on read)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
```

## Story

Big Ted had been in the boys' room for as long as anyone could remember. He was enormous — the kind of enormous where you could lean against him like a sofa, which Felix and Alex often did. He wore a little bow tie and had a very calm face, the face of someone who had seen everything and was not worried about any of it.

Winchester, who came up to Big Ted's elbow, looked at him with great professional respect.

"You're very large," said Winchester.

"Yes," said Big Ted, in a slow, deep, rumbly voice, like a friendly avalanche.

"Do you know things?" said Winchester.

"Most things," said Big Ted. "I've been listening for a long time."

Winchester nodded. This was exactly the sort of ally you wanted.

---

It was Amelia who brought the problem.

She arrived on Saturday morning with her notebook already open, which meant she'd been thinking since at least Thursday. She had a small crease between her eyebrows that meant this was a real problem, not a practice one.

"The town library," she said, "has gone quiet."

"Libraries are quiet," said Felix.

"Not like this," said Amelia. "The words have gone out of the books."

Felix opened his mouth. Closed it. Opened it again. "The words have *gone out of the books*?"

"Every page blank. Every book in the whole library. Overnight." Amelia turned her notebook around. She'd drawn a diagram. "The Library Keeper is very upset."

Alex, who had been listening from the beanbag, said: "All of them?"

"Every single one."

Alex stood up. When Alex stood up quietly, it meant something. "We should go," he said.

Winchester was already putting his boots on. Big Ted rose from the floor with great slow dignity, adjusted his bow tie, and said: "I'll come."

"You're very large for a library," said Amelia.

"I'll be careful," said Big Ted.

---

The town library was a tall old building with stone steps and big wooden doors and a smell of paper and time that Felix loved even on normal days. Today it felt strange — hushed in the wrong way, the way a room feels when something is missing.

The Library Keeper was at her desk. She was a small woman with silver hair piled on top of her head, held in place by two pencils, and she had been the Library Keeper for longer than anyone in town could remember. Some people thought she *was* the library, just in a cardigan.

She looked up when they came in. Her eyes went to Big Ted. Big Ted looked back at her calmly.

"You brought a large bear," she said.

"He knows things," said Winchester, from somewhere around knee height.

The Library Keeper considered this. "I suppose things-knowers are useful today," she said.

She showed them. Every book open, every page empty. Not torn — not damaged — just *blank*, white and smooth as if the words had simply decided to leave. Felix touched a page. It felt normal. The words were gone but the paper remembered them, somehow. Little ghost-shapes where letters used to be.

"Alex," said Felix quietly.

Alex was already moving. He walked very slowly along the shelves, looking at the books the way he looked at everything — carefully, like the answer was already there if you were patient enough. He stopped at the far end, near the window.

"Here," he said.

There was a small device on the windowsill. About the size of a jam jar, made of something that looked like dark glass, and it was *full* — swirling inside with tiny lights, thousands of them, like fireflies. Like letters. Like every word from every book, caught and spinning.

"Someone bottled the words," said Amelia.

"Who?" said Felix.

"Me," said a voice, miserably, from behind the encyclopaedias.

They looked. Crouched in the gap between the shelf and the wall was a boy. He was about nine, with ink-stained fingers and a jumper with a hole in the elbow and the expression of someone who had done something that had gone much further than intended.

"I only wanted *one* story," he said. "I just wanted to borrow a story and my card was cancelled because I returned something late and she said I couldn't borrow anything and I just — the device was supposed to take *one* story. Not all of them."

He looked genuinely horrified at himself.

Felix looked at him. Then at the Library Keeper. "Why was his card cancelled?"

The Library Keeper had the grace to look slightly uncomfortable. "Policy," she said. "Three weeks overdue."

"What was the book?" said Alex.

"*The Enchanted Wood*," said the boy quietly. "I kept renewing it. Then I couldn't. I just — I wanted to keep reading it."

There was a silence.

Big Ted, who had been standing very still near the door because he was being careful about his size, spoke for the first time since they'd arrived. His voice was slow and warm as an old radiator.

"Books," he said, "are meant to be read. That is the only rule that matters."

The Library Keeper looked at Big Ted for a long moment. Then she sat down in her chair. Then she said, in a smaller voice than usual: "The policy is quite strict."

"The policy made him think stealing was the only option," said Amelia, not unkindly. She had her notebook out. She was writing something.

Felix picked up the device from the windowsill very carefully. The words swirled inside. Thousands and thousands of them, all the stories, all the facts, all the poems, round and round.

"How do you put them back?" he asked the boy.

"Unscrew the bottom," said the boy. "But you have to be inside the library when you do it. Otherwise they'll go anywhere."

Felix looked at Alex. Alex nodded.

Felix unscrewed the bottom of the device.

The words came out like a breath — one enormous, wonderful, rushing exhale — a *WHOOOOSH* of letters that spun up to the ceiling and then, as if they knew exactly where they belonged, streamed back to the shelves. Pouring into spines, filling pages, the books riffling their own pages as the words came home. It lasted about thirty seconds and it was one of the most beautiful things Felix had ever seen.

Then it was quiet again. The right kind of quiet this time.

The boy — his name turned out to be Cormac — picked up *The Enchanted Wood* from where it had fallen and held it like something precious.

The Library Keeper stood up. She pulled one of the pencils from her hair and used it to cross something out in her register. Then she stamped a card with a date that was six weeks away.

"Renewable twice," she said, and held it out to Cormac.

Cormac took it. He looked like he might cry, so he looked at the floor instead.

"Sorry," he said. "About all the words."

"You brought them back," said the Library Keeper. "That counts for considerably more."

---

Walking home, Winchester sat on Big Ted's shoulder, which was large enough to sit on comfortably.

"You knew," Winchester said to Big Ted.

"About the words? No," said Big Ted. "But I knew the boy wasn't bad. You can tell. He had ink on his fingers. You only get ink on your fingers if you love books."

Felix thought about this for the rest of the way home.

That night, he got out his own book — the one he'd been reading slowly because he didn't want it to end — and he read three more chapters in one go, which was brave, and he didn't try to make it last.

Some things are better finished and then started again from the beginning.

*The End.*

---

**Postscripts:**

*Cormac finished* The Enchanted Wood *the following Tuesday. He returned it on Wednesday. He is now on the waiting list for the sequel.*

*The Library Keeper quietly revised the overdue policy. Books can now be renewed five times. She told nobody this was a change. Everyone noticed anyway.*

*Winchester filed the word-bottle on the evidence shelf between the echo jar and Gerald's tooth. It's empty now, but sometimes, if you hold it to your ear, you can hear a very faint story.*

*Big Ted came home and went back to his spot and said nothing about the adventure. He never does. But his bow tie was slightly crooked, which for Big Ted is the equivalent of a enormous smile.*

---

**Affirmations woven through:**
- *Loving something isn't the same as being allowed to take it — but it matters that you love it*
- *Rules that make stealing feel like the only option need looking at*
- *You can tell a good person by what they care about*
- *Things are better finished bravely than stretched out from fear*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# Harry Joins the Adventure Club

```meta
id: harry-joins-the-adventure-club
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.45
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 4 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 4
words: 1717
minutes: ~13
date: 2025-12-16
source_chat: Felix and Alex's adventure group
source_uuid: 8d93ba3d-2619-4e19-8e9a-8bace5d78b89
prompt: And then it. And Can we have another story? Alex's best friend, Harry, arrives in town. He's come to live there and join in their adventure club. Let's go.
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: DISCOVERY. Inclusion of a new friend. NB 'Harry' is a one-off guest — check canon fit.
```

## Story

Felix and Alex were playing in their garden when they heard a moving van rumbling down their lane. But this time, instead of feeling sad like when Theo had to move away, Alex felt his heart start beating very fast with excitement.

"Do you think...?" he said to Felix, barely daring to hope.

"Could it be?" Felix replied, watching the van turn into the driveway of the cottage that had been empty since Mrs. Patterson moved to live with her daughter.

Before either of them could say anything else, a familiar voice shouted from across the garden fence: "Alex! Felix! Guess what!"

Both twins spun around to see a boy with messy brown hair and a huge grin climbing over the fence with the confidence of someone who'd done it a thousand times before.

"Harry!" Alex shouted, launching himself at his friend. "You're here! You're actually here!"

Felix watched with interest as his brother hugged the boy he'd heard so much about but never actually met. Harry had been Alex's best friend at their old school before they moved to their current village. They'd written letters and talked on the phone, but they hadn't seen each other for more than a year.

"My dad got a job at the university near here," Harry explained breathlessly, still grinning. "When Mum told me we were moving to your village, I couldn't believe it! We're going to be neighbors again!"

"This is the best news ever!" Alex said, bouncing on his toes with excitement. "You have to meet Theo and Violet - they're our best friends here. And you have to see our Adventure Club headquarters! And we can show you all the secret places we've found!"

Harry turned to Felix with a slightly nervous expression. "You must be Felix. Alex has told me loads about you in his letters."

"All good things, I hope," Felix said with a grin, immediately liking Harry's friendly face.

"Definitely. He said you're the bravest person he knows, and you always think of the best adventures."

---

They found Theo and Violet in the old railway cutting, where they'd been working on a new fort made of branches and fallen logs. Both friends looked up curiously when they saw the three boys approaching.

"Theo! Violet!" Alex called excitedly. "Come and meet Harry! He's my friend from my old school, and he's just moved here!"

Theo and Violet exchanged a glance. They were always polite to new people, but they'd grown quite comfortable as a group of four friends. Adding a fifth person might change everything.

"Hello," Violet said politely, though she stayed close to Theo.

"Hi," Theo added, equally polite but cautious.

Harry seemed to sense their uncertainty. "Alex has told me so much about you both," he said warmly. "You're Theo, the one who tells amazing stories, right? And Violet, you're the brilliant artist who solved the mystery of the buried time capsule?"

Both Theo and Violet looked pleased but still a bit wary.

"That's right," Theo said, puffing up a little with pride.

"Alex says you're building an Adventure Club," Harry continued. "That sounds really cool. I love adventures."

"What kind of adventures?" Violet asked, testing him.

Harry thought for a moment. "Well, at my old house, I found a family of hedgehogs living under our shed, and I built them a proper hedgehog hotel with different rooms. And once I followed a stream for miles to see where it went, and I discovered an old waterwheel that nobody had used for years."

Theo's eyes lit up with interest despite himself. "A real waterwheel? What did you do with it?"

"I cleaned all the weeds away and got it turning again. It made the most amazing sound - like wooden music."

Felix found himself getting excited too. "We have an old mill wheel here! We found it when we were solving the mystery of Bramble Hollow."

"Could you show me?" Harry asked eagerly.

And just like that, the ice began to break.

---

But over the next few days, things felt a bit complicated. Harry was lovely - funny, adventurous, and genuinely interested in all the places and games that were special to their group. The problem was that Alex was so excited to have his old friend back that he kept wanting to do everything with Harry, sometimes forgetting to include the others.

"Let's show Harry the secret path through the woods," Alex would say. "Just the two of us - we can tell him about all our old adventures from before we moved here."

Or, "Harry and I are going to practice climbing trees. We used to have competitions at our old school."

Felix, Theo, and Violet found themselves feeling left out, even though Alex didn't mean to exclude them.

"It's like Alex has forgotten we exist," Violet said sadly as the three of them sat in their Adventure Club headquarters while Alex and Harry explored the village together.

"He's just excited," Felix said, though he felt hurt too. "Harry was his friend first, before any of us."

"Maybe there's not room for five people in our group," Theo said quietly. "Maybe four was the perfect number."

They sat in silence for a while, each thinking about how much their friendship group had changed in just one week.

---

The situation came to a head when Harry suggested they should rename their Adventure Club.

"What's wrong with the name we have?" Violet asked, bristling.

"Nothing! It's just that since I'm new, maybe we could think of a name that includes everyone equally," Harry explained. "Like the Five Friends Club, or the Village Explorers, or something."

"But we've always been the Adventure Club," Theo said firmly. "It's our name."

"And you've only been here for a week," Violet added, not trying very hard to be polite anymore.

Harry looked hurt and confused. "I wasn't trying to take over. I just thought..."

"You thought you could come here and change everything to suit you," Theo finished.

"That's not fair," Alex protested, defending his friend. "Harry's just trying to fit in."

"By changing everything we've built together?" Felix asked quietly.

The five friends stood in an awkward circle, all feeling misunderstood and frustrated.

"Maybe this isn't working," Harry said sadly. "Maybe I should just find my own friends."

"Maybe you should," Violet said, though she looked like she regretted the words as soon as they came out of her mouth.

---

That evening, Alex sat in his bedroom feeling terrible. His two worlds - his old friendship with Harry and his new friendship with Felix, Theo, and Violet - were crashing into each other in all the wrong ways.

Felix found him staring out the window toward Harry's house.

"You know," Felix said, sitting down on Alex's bed, "I don't think anyone's being fair to anyone else."

"What do you mean?"

"Harry's trying really hard to fit in, but Theo and Violet aren't giving him a proper chance because they're worried he'll take you away from us. And you're so excited about having Harry back that you're not noticing how left out we feel."

Alex felt his cheeks go hot with shame. "I didn't mean to leave you out."

"I know. But you did anyway."

They sat quietly for a moment.

"What should we do?" Alex asked.

"I think," Felix said thoughtfully, "we need to start over. All of us."

---

The next morning, Alex knocked on Harry's door with a nervous expression.

"I owe you an apology," he said. "I've been so excited about you being here that I forgot about my other friends' feelings. And I should have helped you understand how our group works instead of just expecting you to figure it out."

Harry looked relieved. "I owe everyone an apology too. I was trying so hard to be included that I didn't respect the things that were already special to your group."

"Want to try again?" Alex asked.

"Definitely."

They found Felix, Theo, and Violet sitting under the apple tree, looking equally miserable.

"We want to apologize," Alex announced. "Both of us."

"We've been thinking," Harry added, "and we realize we've been doing everything wrong."

"How do you mean?" Theo asked cautiously.

"Well," Alex said, "I was so excited about Harry being here that I forgot how important you all are to me. And instead of helping everyone get to know each other properly, I kept trying to have separate friendships."

"And I was so worried about fitting in," Harry continued, "that I tried to change things instead of learning to appreciate them the way they were."

Violet looked interested despite herself. "So what do you suggest?"

"We start completely over," Alex said. "Harry, meet the Adventure Club. Adventure Club, meet Harry. No rushing, no changing anything, just getting to know each other properly."

---

And that's exactly what they did. Instead of trying to force Harry into their existing group, they took the time to let him learn about their traditions and special places. Harry shared stories about his old adventures, but he also listened carefully to their stories and showed genuine respect for the things that mattered to them.

Slowly, naturally, Harry began to find his place in the group. It turned out he was excellent at building things, which complemented Theo's storytelling, Violet's creativity, Felix's bravery, and Alex's careful planning.

"You know what?" Violet said one afternoon as all five friends worked together to build the most elaborate fort they'd ever constructed. "Five friends isn't too many after all."

"It's just different," Theo agreed. "But good different."

"Really good different," Felix added. "We can do bigger projects now, and go on more complex adventures."

"And there's always someone to be your partner when we split into teams," Alex observed.

Harry smiled as he carefully fitted another branch into their fort wall. "Thanks for giving me a second chance. I really love being part of the Adventure Club."

"Thanks for being patient while we figured out how to share our friendship," Violet replied.

And they all agreed that sometimes the best friendships were the ones that had to stretch and grow to make room for new people, because the right friends always made the group stronger, not weaker.

---

*The End*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Secret of Miller's Cottage

```meta
id: the-secret-of-millers-cottage
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.45
scores: affirm 4 / emotion 5 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 5
words: 1992
minutes: ~15
date: 2025-12-16
source_chat: Felix and Alex's adventure group
source_uuid: 8d93ba3d-2619-4e19-8e9a-8bace5d78b89
prompt: Please tell me another story like this with Felix, Alex, Theo, and Violet.
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: DISCOVERY. Longer grounded mystery. Read to confirm.
```

## Story

It was a rainy Tuesday afternoon when Mrs. Pemberton from the village library knocked on their door, her usually cheerful face creased with worry.

"I'm so sorry to bother you," she said to Mum, wringing her hands. "But I wondered if the children had seen Biscuit anywhere? He's been missing for three days now."

Felix and Alex knew Biscuit well—the library's enormous ginger cat who spent his days sleeping on the sunny windowsill and greeting visitors with loud purrs. Every time they visited for story time, Biscuit would wind around their legs and follow them to the children's section.

"He's never been away this long," Mrs. Pemberton continued. "I've put up posters, and I've searched everywhere I can think of. I just thought maybe..."

"We'll help look for him," Felix said immediately, appearing at Mum's elbow. "Won't we, Alex?"

Alex nodded seriously. He could see how upset Mrs. Pemberton was, and besides, he'd always liked Biscuit. The cat was clever—he seemed to know which children were nervous about reading aloud and would always settle quietly beside them.

"That's very kind," Mrs. Pemberton said, managing a small smile. "He was wearing his blue collar with the little bell. If you see him..."

"We'll bring him straight back," Alex promised.

---

After Mrs. Pemberton left, the twins sat in the kitchen making a plan. The rain drummed against the windows, and everything outside looked grey and miserable.

"Where would you go if you were a cat?" Felix mused, drawing circles on his foggy breath on the window.

"Somewhere warm and dry," Alex said practically. "With food."

"Or somewhere quiet, where nobody would bother you," Felix added. "Cats like to hide when they're scared."

They decided to start by asking Theo and Violet if they'd seen Biscuit, then search all the obvious places—the bakery (which always smelled like warm bread), the butcher's shop (which might have scraps), and the little lanes between the old cottages where cats often sunbathed.

---

Theo hadn't seen Biscuit, but he had a theory.

"My gran says cats sometimes go off when they're not feeling well," he said, pulling on his wellies in his hallway. "They find a quiet place to rest until they feel better."

"But where would be quiet around here?" Violet asked, joining them with her bright yellow raincoat buttoned up to her chin.

They stood in Theo's garden, looking out over the village. Most of the houses were close together, with busy little gardens and people coming and going. But at the edge of the village, past the church and the post office, stood Miller's Cottage.

It had been empty for months, ever since old Mr. Miller had moved to live with his daughter in town. The cottage sat by itself beside the stream that used to turn the mill wheel, surrounded by overgrown hedges and tall grass.

"There," said Alex, pointing. "If I was a cat and I wanted to be left alone, that's where I'd go."

"But it's all locked up," Violet said doubtfully.

Felix was already heading for the gate. "Come on. Let's at least look."

---

Miller's Cottage looked sad in the rain. The windows were dark, and ivy was beginning to creep up the walls. The garden that Mr. Miller had kept so neat was now a tangle of weeds and long grass. A "For Sale" sign leaned drunkenly by the front gate.

"Biscuit!" Felix called, cupping his hands around his mouth. "Here, Biscuit!"

They walked slowly around the cottage, calling and listening. The only sounds were the rain pattering on leaves and the stream babbling over the stones of the old mill wheel.

But then Alex noticed something.

"Look," he said quietly, pointing to one of the downstairs windows. "The curtain moved."

They all stared at the window. For a moment, nothing happened. Then, very slightly, the edge of the curtain twitched.

"There's someone in there," Violet whispered.

Theo's eyes went wide. "Maybe it's a burglar!"

"Or maybe," Felix said, his voice getting excited, "it's Biscuit. Maybe he got inside somehow."

Alex was thinking. "The cottage has been empty for months. If someone's in there who shouldn't be..."

"We should tell a grown-up," Violet said firmly.

But Felix was already walking toward the window. "What if it's just Biscuit? What if he's stuck in there and can't get out?"

He pressed his face to the glass, shading his eyes with his hands to see better. The others crowded around him.

The room inside was mostly empty, with just a few pieces of old furniture covered in dust sheets. But in the corner, curled up on what looked like an old blanket, was a familiar ginger shape.

"It's him!" Felix said triumphantly. "It's Biscuit!"

The cat looked up at the sound of Felix's voice, and they could see his mouth open in what was probably a meow, though they couldn't hear it through the glass.

"But how did he get in?" Alex wondered. "And how do we get him out?"

---

They walked around the cottage again, looking more carefully this time. All the windows were shut tight, and the front and back doors were locked with big brass padlocks. But when they got to the back of the house, where the old mill wheel stood silent in the stream, Violet spotted something.

"There," she said, pointing to a small window near the ground. "That one's open."

It was barely open—just a crack—but it was definitely not shut properly. The window was tiny, probably leading to what used to be Mr. Miller's pantry.

"Biscuit could have squeezed through there," Alex said. "But it's too small for us."

"And too high up," Theo added. The window was about four feet off the ground.

Felix was already looking around for something to stand on. "If we could just open it wider..."

"Wait," said Violet. She was staring at the window with a thoughtful expression. "There's something else."

She pointed to the windowsill. There, barely visible in the rain, were muddy marks that looked like they might have been made by boots.

"Someone else has climbed up there," Alex said slowly.

"Recently," Violet added. "Those marks are fresh."

They looked at each other uneasily.

"Maybe someone's been feeding Biscuit," Theo suggested hopefully.

"Or maybe," Alex said, "someone's been using the cottage when they're not supposed to."

---

The four friends stood in the rain, trying to decide what to do. Biscuit was definitely inside—they could see him at the window now, pawing at the glass. But if someone else was using the empty cottage...

"We have to tell someone," Violet said again. "This time I really mean it."

Alex nodded. "But who? And what do we say? We don't actually know anything for sure."

"We know Biscuit's in there," Felix pointed out.

"And we know someone's been at that window," Violet added.

Theo was quiet for a moment, thinking. Then he said, "My gran knows Mr. Miller. She might have a key, or know who does."

"And she'd believe us," Alex agreed. "She wouldn't think we were being silly."

So they splashed back through the puddles to Theo's house, where his grandmother was in the kitchen making jam and listening to the radio.

"Biscuit's in Miller's Cottage," Theo announced without preamble. "And someone else might be using it too."

Theo's gran—who everyone called Nana Rose—listened carefully as they explained everything they'd seen. She didn't interrupt or tell them they were imagining things, which was one of the reasons all four children liked her so much.

When they finished, she wiped her hands on her apron and reached for the phone.

"I think," she said, "that we need to call both Mrs. Pemberton and the police. Just to be safe."

---

PC Williams arrived first, followed quickly by Mrs. Pemberton in her little red car. Nana Rose made everyone tea while the children told their story again, and PC Williams took notes in his official notebook.

"You were right to call," he told them. "Empty properties sometimes attract people who shouldn't be there. Even if it's just someone sheltering from the weather, we need to know about it."

He asked Nana Rose if she had a key to the cottage. She didn't, but she knew that Mrs. Henderson from the post office was looking after things for Mr. Miller while the cottage was for sale.

Twenty minutes later, they all trooped back to Miller's Cottage in a small procession—PC Williams, Mrs. Pemberton, Nana Rose, and the four children. Mrs. Henderson met them there with a large ring of keys.

"I do check on the place once a week," she said, looking worried. "But I haven't been since Sunday because of the rain."

They went to the back door first, and Mrs. Henderson unlocked the padlock with shaking hands. The door swung open to reveal the cottage's small back kitchen, which smelled of damp and neglect.

"Biscuit!" Mrs. Pemberton called. "Biscuit, where are you?"

A loud meow answered her, and the ginger cat appeared in the doorway to the next room, looking bedraggled but otherwise fine. Mrs. Pemberton scooped him up immediately, burying her face in his wet fur.

"Oh, you silly boy," she murmured. "You had us all so worried."

But PC Williams was looking around the kitchen with a professional eye. On the old table were some things that definitely didn't belong to Mr. Miller: a thermos flask, some sandwiches wrapped in plastic, and a torch.

"Someone's been here recently," he said. "Very recently."

---

It turned out to be less dramatic than they'd feared. PC Williams found evidence that someone had been using the cottage as a place to sleep—probably someone homeless who had nowhere else to go. There were blankets in the front room and tins of food in the kitchen. Everything was tidy and nothing was damaged.

"Looks like they were trying to be respectful," PC Williams said. "But they still shouldn't have been here without permission."

The mystery of how Biscuit got in was solved when they found the little pantry window, which someone had jimmied open from the outside. The cat had probably smelled food and squeezed through, then couldn't figure out how to get back out.

"He's put on weight," Mrs. Pemberton observed, holding Biscuit at arm's length. "Someone's been feeding him well."

"Probably whoever was staying here," PC Williams agreed. "Some people are kind even when they're in difficult situations."

Mrs. Henderson said she'd make sure the window was properly secured, and PC Williams said he'd keep an eye out for whoever had been using the cottage, to try to help them find proper accommodation.

---

That evening, as the rain finally stopped and the sun came out, the four friends sat in Theo's garden sharing a bag of sweets and feeling pleased with themselves.

"We found Biscuit," Felix said contentedly.

"We solved a mystery," Alex added.

"Even if it wasn't a scary mystery," Theo said. "Just someone who needed help."

Violet was sketching in her notebook again, trying to capture the moment when Mrs. Pemberton had hugged Biscuit in the cottage doorway.

"I'm glad whoever it was looked after him," she said. "That was kind."

"Nana Rose says kindness counts, even when you're doing something you shouldn't," Theo said.

Mrs. Pemberton had invited them all to the library the next day for a special story time with Biscuit, to celebrate his safe return. She'd also given them each a book to keep, for being "excellent detectives and caring friends."

Alex looked at his new book—a collection of real-life mysteries suitable for children. "I wonder what we'll investigate next."

"Something will turn up," Felix said confidently. "Adventures always do, if you keep your eyes open."

And as they sat there in the golden evening light, with the excitement of their successful detective work still warm in their chests, they all had the feeling that Felix was probably right.

---

*The End*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Adventure of the Missing Cake

```meta
id: the-adventure-of-the-missing-cake
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.45
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 4 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 4
words: 1340
minutes: ~10
date: 2026-07-01
source_chat: The missing cake fairy adventure
source_uuid: a7b5059f-af19-4884-a755-86643a023b3c
prompt: another story about all the Alex's you might need to search other stories to find context there are 4 Alex's I think.   new adventure, Alex's Felix and Amelia, everyone's a fairy, 
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: Empathy for hungry hedgehog; fix-a-mistake-kindly.
```

## Story

### *A Fairy Story for Felix, Alex and Amelia*

---

It started with a smell.

Alex noticed it first — he always noticed things first — a warm, sugary, buttery smell drifting across the fifteen-acre field.

"That's Mummy's cake," he said. "The one for the fairy picnic."

They looked toward the kitchen window. Then they looked at each other. Then they looked down.

Somewhere between the window and this exact moment, both of them had grown wings.

Small, glittery, slightly wobbly wings, catching the afternoon light like soap bubbles.

"FELIX," said Felix, delighted with himself. "I'M A FAIRY."

"We're both fairies," said Alex, examining his wings with great seriousness, the way he examined everything.

A little shower of gold dust drifted past their noses, and there was Amelia, hovering just above the garden gate, her own wings a deep, sensible blue, already looking rather pleased about the whole situation.

"I did wonder if this would happen today," she said, landing neatly. "I brought my notebook. Just in case."

"You always bring your notebook," said Felix.

"Yes," said Amelia. "That's why it's always there when we need it."

---

Being a fairy, it turned out, came with a wonderful discovery: they could *make* things. Not find them, not wish for them — actually shape them out of the sparkle in the air, just by knowing exactly what they needed.

Felix waved his hand and, in a small **FWOOMP** of glitter, found himself holding a tiny golden sword, perfectly sized for a fairy hand.

"Whoa!" He swished it. It made a satisfying *zzzt* sound.

Alex thought for a moment, then closed his eyes. A pair of small round spectacles appeared on his nose, the lenses swirling faintly like they were made of fog.

"What do they do?" asked Felix.

"I don't know yet," said Alex. He put them on. Everything nearby went slightly blurry, except for one thing — a single set of tiny footprints in the flowerbed, glowing faintly gold. "Oh. They show me where things have *been*."

Amelia's notebook had grown a small feathered fairy quill, which wrote things down all by itself the moment she thought them clearly enough.

"Right," she said, already writing. "Missing cake. Fairy powers. Let's find out what happened."

*Remember: you don't need to know everything before you start. You just need to start.*

---

They flew — actually flew, low over the grass, wings humming — to the kitchen window, where Daddy was standing looking very confused indeed, and rather smaller than usual, which meant of course that they had shrunk, not him.

"The cake was RIGHT HERE," Robert was saying to nobody in particular. "I turned around for one minute to put the kettle on—"

"Kettle's still a good idea," said Felix loyally, from the windowsill.

Robert looked down and did a small double-take at the two fairies and their older fairy friend perched on his windowsill.

"Ah," he said. "You've noticed the wings, then."

"WE'RE FAIRIES," said Felix, in case this wasn't clear.

"So I see. Well. I'll make the tea. You three go and sort this out — you're clearly better equipped than I am." He held up his enormous, wingless hands as evidence.

*That's the thing about grown-ups. Sometimes the bravest thing they do is let you handle it yourself.*

---

Alex put his spectacles on and looked down at the windowsill. The golden footprints picked up again here — tiny, careful, a little bit wobbly, heading off across the garden toward the hedge.

"This way," he said. "Whoever took it was small. And it walked funny near the end. Like it was carrying something heavy."

"Or something delicious," said Felix.

They followed the glowing trail past the roses, under the gate, and into a gap in the hedge none of them had ever noticed before — which of course meant it was fairy-sized, and had probably been there the whole time, waiting to be seen by the right sort of eyes.

*Some doors only open when you're ready to find them.*

---

On the other side was a tiny mossy clearing, and sitting in the middle of it, looking extremely guilty, was the smallest hedgehog any of them had ever seen — no bigger than Amelia's thumb — sitting next to a cake that was, comparatively, the size of a mountain.

He had one paw buried in the icing.

"Oh," said Alex.

The hedgehog froze. Then, seeing three fairies looking at him, he burst into tears, which is a very small and very sad sound when you are a very small hedgehog.

"I didn't mean to take the whole thing," he sniffled. "It smelled so good and I only meant to have a *little* taste and then I couldn't stop and then it was too heavy to put back and I don't even *have* anywhere to put a whole cake and now everyone will think I'm a thief and—"

"Hey," said Felix, landing right in front of him, sword put away, because you don't need a sword for a crying hedgehog. "You're not a thief. You're just really hungry and it smelled amazing. That's not the same thing."

*Making a mistake doesn't make you a bad person. It just makes you a person.*

The hedgehog sniffed. "Really?"

"Really," said Alex, sitting down next to him, spectacles pushed up onto his head. "I bet you didn't have breakfast, or lunch, or a nice cosy place to keep warm. That's why you took it. Not because you're naughty."

The hedgehog nodded miserably. "There's a hole in my log. The rain gets in."

Amelia had already written three lines in her notebook and was now looking thoughtfully at the cake.

"Well," she said. "There's rather a lot of cake here. Far too much for a picnic *and* not enough for a hungry hedgehog with a leaky log. I think the sensible thing is for everyone to have some."

*You can fix a mistake and still be kind about it at the same time. Both things can be true.*

---

Felix waved his glittery hand and — **FWOOMP** — a tiny golden trowel appeared, exactly hedgehog-sized.

"For patching your log," he said, presenting it grandly.

Alex closed his eyes and thought very hard about warm and dry and cosy, and a small knitted blanket, soft and red, folded itself out of the sparkle in the air.

"For the rain," he said, tucking it around the hedgehog's shoulders.

The hedgehog looked at the sword-boy and the spectacles-boy and the notebook-girl, and at his own paw, still a bit sticky with icing, and he smiled properly for the first time.

"Thank you," he whispered. "I'm Bramble."

"I'm Felix. That's Alex. That's Amelia."

"Are you going to tell your mummy it was me?"

Amelia considered this seriously, the way she considered most things.

"We're going to tell her the cake found a very good use," she said. "That's not the same as a lie. It's just the important part."

---

They carried the rest of the cake home between the three of them — flying is much easier when something is shared, it turns out — and left a fat slice, wrapped in a leaf, tucked into Bramble's newly patched log.

Back in the kitchen, Robert had made the tea exactly on time, as he always did.

"Solved it?" he asked.

"Solved it," said Felix.

"Fed a hedgehog," said Alex.

"Wrote it all down," said Amelia, closing her notebook with a satisfied little *snap*.

By the time the tea had cooled enough to drink, the wings had gone — quietly, the way fairy things do — and it was just two boys and a girl at the kitchen table, one slice of cake short, and none of them minding one bit.

---

🌟 **THE END** 🌟

---

*Bramble's log stays dry now.*
*He leaves tiny golden footprints by the back step some mornings, just to say hello.*
*Alex always notices them first.*
*Nobody's surprised by that anymore.*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Polish Adventure

```meta
id: the-polish-adventure
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.424
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 3.92 / enjoy 3.2 / audio 5.0 / craft 5
words: 1371
minutes: ~10
date: 2025-12-16
source_chat: Felix and Alex's adventure group
source_uuid: 8d93ba3d-2619-4e19-8e9a-8bace5d78b89
prompt: Can you tell us a different story? Where Felix and Alex are going to move to Poland? Where their grandparents are from. And still live.
reviewed: auto-scored (confirm on read)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
```

## Story

Felix and Alex were sitting at the kitchen table eating their breakfast when Mum and Dad gave them the biggest news of their entire lives.

"We have something very important to tell you," Dad said, sitting down with his serious face on. "You know how we've been talking about Babcia and Dziadek getting older?"

Felix and Alex nodded. Babcia and Dziadek were their Polish grandparents who lived far away in a city called Krakow. They'd visited twice, but it took a whole day on airplanes to get there.

"Well," Mum continued, "Babcia isn't feeling very well, and Dziadek needs help looking after her. So we've decided to move to Poland to be closer to them."

Felix's spoon stopped halfway to his mouth. "Move to Poland? Forever?"

"For a few years, at least," Dad explained. "Maybe longer. We want to help take care of your grandparents, and we think it would be wonderful for you to learn Polish properly and understand that part of your family."

Alex felt his tummy go all fluttery. "But what about our friends? What about school? What about our house?"

"We know it's a big change," Mum said gently. "But sometimes families need to help each other, even when it means doing difficult things."

The twins looked at each other with the same terrible thought: How could they possibly tell Theo and Violet that they were moving to the other side of the world?

---

They found their friends building a den in Violet's garden, chattering happily about their plans for the summer holidays.

"We could have camping adventures every weekend," Theo was saying excitedly. "And maybe we could learn to build a proper treehouse."

"With windows made of colored plastic," Violet added, "so the light inside is all rainbow-y."

Felix and Alex stood at the garden gate, feeling like they were about to drop a bomb on their friends' happiness.

"Theo, Violet," Alex said quietly. "We need to tell you something important."

Both friends looked up immediately, sensing from the twins' voices that something serious was happening.

"What is it?" Violet asked, her paintbrush dripping blue paint onto the grass.

"We have to move away," Felix said in a rush. "To Poland. Where our grandparents live."

For a moment, nobody said anything. Theo and Violet just stared at them with their mouths open.

"Move away?" Theo repeated slowly. "Like, far away?"

"Very far away," Alex confirmed miserably. "It takes a whole day to travel there."

Violet's eyes filled with tears. "But you can't move away. You live here. This is where you belong."

"Our grandparents need help," Felix explained. "And Mum and Dad want us to learn about being Polish."

"When?" Theo asked in a very small voice.

"In six weeks," Alex whispered. "Before the new school year starts."

---

The news felt too big and too sad for any of them to know what to do with it. They sat in Violet's half-finished den, not building or playing or talking, just trying to understand that their group of four best friends was about to become two and two, separated by hundreds of miles.

"Will you learn to speak Polish?" Violet asked eventually.

"We already know some words," Felix said. "Babcia and Dziadek taught us. 'Dzień dobry' means 'good day' and 'kocham cię' means 'I love you.'"

"Will you go to a Polish school?" Theo wondered.

"Yes, but Mum says lots of children there speak English too, because they learn it like we learn French here."

"Will you live in a different house?"

"We're going to live with Babcia and Dziadek at first," Alex explained. "Their house has a big garden and it's near a castle that's hundreds and hundreds of years old."

Despite feeling sad, Theo and Violet had to admit that living near a real castle sounded quite exciting.

"Maybe," Violet said slowly, "it will be like an adventure. A really big adventure."

"But we don't want an adventure without you," Felix said sadly. "Adventures are only fun when you're with your best friends."

---

Over the next few weeks, the four friends tried to fit in a whole lifetime of adventures. They visited every special place they'd ever discovered together - the secret path to Bramble Hollow, the old railway cutting where they'd imagined magical trains, Mrs. Weatherby's garden where they'd found the buried time capsule.

Theo made them a book of all the stories they'd shared, writing each one down carefully and illustrating it with drawings that Violet helped him make. Violet painted portraits of all four of them together, one for each friend to keep forever.

Felix and Alex tried to learn as many Polish words as possible so they could teach them to Theo and Violet.

"This is how you say 'hello,'" Alex explained. "'Cześć.' And this is how you say 'friend' - 'przyjaciel.'"

"Cheshch," Theo attempted. "Pshee-ya-chel."

"Close enough," Felix said with a smile.

They also made lists of everything they wanted to remember about their village, their favorite games, and the things that made their friendship special.

"Promise you'll write letters," Violet said as they sat in their final Best Friends Club meeting.

"Every week," the twins promised.

"And promise you'll tell us about the castle and the Polish food and all the new things you discover."

"We promise."

"And promise you won't forget us when you make new Polish friends."

"Never," Felix and Alex said together. "You're our very best friends forever, no matter where we live."

---

The last day came much too quickly. The moving van had taken most of their belongings the day before, and their house looked strange and empty with just suitcases and a few last things waiting to be packed.

Theo and Violet came to say goodbye, bringing one final gift - a photo album Violet had made with pictures of all their adventures together.

"So you can show your Polish friends who your English friends are," she explained.

"And so you can remember all the fun things we did together," Theo added.

The goodbye hugs lasted a very long time, and everyone cried a little bit, even though they were trying to be brave.

"Take care of each other," Alex said to Theo and Violet. "Don't let being just two friends stop you from having adventures."

"Take care of your grandparents," Violet replied. "And learn lots of Polish words to teach us when we see you again."

"When will we see you again?" Theo asked.

"I don't know," Felix admitted. "But we will. Somehow, someday, we will."

As the taxi arrived to take them to the airport, the twins waved goodbye to their friends, their house, and their village, feeling scared and excited and sad all at the same time.

---

Six months later, when the first snow fell in Krakow, Felix and Alex sat down to write their weekly letter to Theo and Violet.

*Dear Theo and Violet,*

*Poland is amazing! We're getting much better at speaking Polish now, and we've made friends at our new school. There's a girl called Kasia who reminds us of Violet because she loves to paint, and a boy called Tomek who tells jokes like Theo does.*

*But the best thing is that Babcia is feeling better now that we're here to help. She's teaching us to make pierogi (Polish dumplings) and telling us stories about when Mum was little.*

*We miss you every day, but we're learning that you can be happy in a new place and still miss your old friends. And guess what? Mum and Dad say you can come and visit us next summer if your parents say yes! We can show you the castle and teach you Polish words and you can meet our new friends.*

*We hope you're having good adventures together. Write soon and tell us everything!*

*Love from your Polish friends,*
*Felix and Alex*

*P.S. We still have all your pictures on our bedroom wall, and we tell everyone about our best English friends.*

And thousands of miles away in their English village, Theo and Violet read the letter and smiled, because they knew that the very best friendships could stretch across countries and continents without ever breaking.

---

*The End*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Case of the Colour That Went Quiet

```meta
id: the-case-of-the-colour-that-went-quiet
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.42
scores: affirm 4.78 / emotion 5 / enjoy 3.2 / audio 4.0 / craft 4.71
words: 2200
minutes: ~16
date: 2026-05-06
source_chat: Alex's family story
source_uuid: 5a44468f-69ee-4f9a-8900-8d4cd534f9dd
prompt: new story Alex is the primary character, Felix and Amelia, and Evi and Isla and Alex Evi's mum, and Amelia's dad Alex. surprise us
reviewed: auto-scored (confirm on read)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
```

## Story

*A Trio Force Adventure*
*Primary: Alex · With: Felix, Amelia, Evy, Isla, Alex (Evy's mum), and Alex (Amelia's dad)*

---

It started, as things often did, with Alex noticing something before he could explain why it bothered him.

They were all in the garden — Felix doing spectacular rolling dives across the grass for no particular reason, Evy and Isla making a complicated map of something in the mud, Amelia sitting on the wall writing in her notebook — when Alex went very still.

He looked at the fifteen-acre field.

He looked at it for a long time.

"The yellow's gone quiet," he said.

Everyone looked at the field. The field was full of buttercups. They were yellow. They looked fine.

"They look fine," said Felix, completing a particularly excellent dive into a bush.

"They *look* yellow," said Alex. "But they've gone quiet."

Evy looked up from her map immediately. She understood this kind of thing. She pressed one finger to the grass beside her — not the buttercups, just the regular grass — and her eyes went distant the way they did when she was touch-reading.

"Something happened here," she said. "Something passed through. Very recently. It smelled like a library."

"Libraries don't smell," said Felix.

"Yes they do," said Alex, Amelia, and Isla at the same time.

---

That was when the boxes arrived.

Four of them. Gold. Floating in from the direction of the field in a neat little line, like ducks.

"Saturday boxes," breathed Felix. "But it's Wednesday."

"Wednesday can have gold boxes," said Amelia. She was already writing in her notebook. "If it's important enough."

The boxes settled in front of them on the garden wall. One each: Alex, Felix, Amelia, and Evy. Isla put her hands on her hips and looked at hers pointedly, and a fifth box wobbled out of the field, slightly apologetically, and landed in front of her.

Isla nodded. "Thank you."

They opened them.

Felix got **VOICE OF VERY LOUD REASONABLE PERSUASION** — anything he said out loud in a very loud, very reasonable voice would be incredibly difficult to argue with, even if what he was saying was slightly silly.

Amelia got **THE COMPLETE NOTEBOOK** — whatever she wrote in her notebook would come true for exactly seventeen seconds. Enough to test a theory. She looked at this one carefully.

Evy got **EXTENDED TOUCH-HISTORY** — instead of just a moment's history, she could now read the *whole* story of any object she touched. She held her own hand and blinked. "Oh. Interesting."

Isla got **WARM WELCOME** — anyone she smiled at would immediately feel comfortable enough to tell her one true thing.

And Alex.

Alex's box contained a small mirror, no bigger than his palm. It had a silver frame with tiny letters running around the edge that said: *THE QUIET MIRROR — WHAT CANNOT SPEAK, THIS SHOWS.*

He held it up.

The buttercups in the field appeared in the glass.

And in the mirror, they were grey.

---

"Something is taking the colour out of things," said Amelia. "But only the part that can't be seen. The quiet part."

"Colours have a quiet part?" said Felix.

"Everything has a quiet part," said Alex. He was still looking at the mirror. "The bit underneath. The part that makes it feel like what it is."

Felix thought about this very hard. "Like how the kitchen smells like Mummy even when she's not in it?"

Everyone was quiet for a moment.

"Yes," said Alex. "Exactly like that."

They followed the direction the smell-like-a-library had come from, out through the gate, across the fifteen-acre field. The buttercups looked fine. In Alex's mirror, they were getting quieter and quieter — greyer and greyer — as they walked further in.

Evy knelt and pressed both palms flat against the earth.

"A person," she said. "Walking very slowly. Very old shoes. He's been here before, years and years ago, but that time he was running." She paused. "He was happy, that time."

"Not now?" said Isla.

Evy shook her head.

---

They found him at the far end of the field, where the stone bridge crossed into the forest.

He was sitting on the wall of the bridge, which was very unusual because everyone knew you were supposed to cross quietly so as not to bother the Grumble. He had round glasses, a large beige coat with several pens in the pocket, and the air of someone who had forgotten to eat lunch and possibly also breakfast.

He was painting.

Or — he was trying to.

He had a small tin of watercolours, the proper kind with a little brush and everything. He was dabbing at a piece of paper. The strokes were all correct. The colours were all the right colours.

The painting looked completely, entirely, bafflingly grey.

Alex held up the mirror. In it, the man appeared as a swirling fog of very quiet blues and greens — someone who used to feel a lot, and had stopped being able to.

"He's not taking the colour," Alex said quietly. "He's *leaking*."

Amelia's pencil was already moving. "When someone loses their colour-underneath, it drains out of them like water. And everything nearby gets drained too." She looked up. "He doesn't know he's doing it."

"That's so sad," said Isla.

She walked straight up to the man.

"Hello," she said, and she gave him her biggest, warmest smile.

The man blinked. He looked at her the way people do when someone is unexpectedly kind to them and they don't quite know what to do with it.

"Hello," he said. His voice was the colour of old paper.

"What are you painting?" said Isla.

"I'm trying to paint the field," said the man. "I painted it once before. I was about your age. I came back to paint it again." He looked at his paper. "But it won't come."

"What's your name?" said Isla, and she smiled again — the really warm one.

"Marcus," he said, and then, because of the WARM WELCOME: "I used to love painting. I don't think I'm allowed to love it anymore."

"Who said?" said Felix, very loudly and very reasonably.

Marcus blinked. "No one. It's just — I'm quite old. You get to a point where you stop thinking you're allowed."

"THAT IS COMPLETELY WRONG," said Felix, in the most loudly reasonable voice anyone had heard in the fifteen-acre field. "YOU ARE ABSOLUTELY ALLOWED."

It was very difficult to argue with.

Marcus looked confused and hopeful at the same time.

---

"I need to talk to my dad," said Amelia quietly.

She called him on the Trio Force channel. In two minutes, a man in a slightly muddy jacket appeared from the direction of the road, slightly out of breath but not complaining about it. Amelia's dad was called Alex, which always made people do a double-take when he was around Alex the boy, and he handled this with extremely good humour. He was tall, read a great deal, and had the specific energy of someone who found the world endlessly interesting and was happy to let other people lead.

"Dad," said Amelia. "This is Marcus. He's lost the underneath of his colour."

Amelia's dad looked at Marcus for a moment. He had a gift for reading situations and an even better gift for not making a fuss about it.

"Marcus," he said. "I'm Alex. Would you like to sit on the bridge properly rather than the wall? The Grumble doesn't mind if you're very polite about it." He paused. "Also I have a flask."

Marcus stood up from the wall. "Tea?"

"Obviously tea."

---

While Amelia's dad sat with Marcus and listened — properly listened, the way grown-ups so rarely do — the children got to work.

Evy touched Marcus's paint tin. Her eyes went wide and soft. "He used this tin when he was young. He painted hedgerows. His mum was beside him." She set it down carefully.

"He just needs to remember it was real," said Alex. "The colour that was there before. It went quiet because he stopped believing in it, but it didn't go away."

He held up the quiet mirror.

In it, he could see Marcus — still a swirl of grey fog — but underneath, there were colours. Deep blue and warm ochre and the specific green of summer grass. They were very small. But they were there.

Alex thought.

He turned the mirror over.

On the back, in the same tiny silver letters: *WHAT CANNOT SPEAK, THIS SHOWS — BUT FIRST YOU MUST BELIEVE IT LOUD ENOUGH TO HEAR.*

Alex showed it to Felix.

Felix nodded seriously, in the manner of someone who absolutely understands.

Then Felix walked over to Marcus and Amelia's dad.

"Excuse me," said Felix. He was not using the power. This was just Felix. "I think you need to know something."

Marcus looked at him.

"The painting you did when you were small," said Felix. "It's still real. Your mum was there. The hedgerows were there. You being happy was there. That doesn't stop being true just because it was a long time ago." He paused. "Alex told me colours have a quiet part. I think yours just needs someone to be loud about it for a bit until it remembers how to be loud for itself."

Marcus was very still.

Amelia's dad put a cup of tea into Marcus's hands.

Isla smiled at Marcus one more time — the full warmth of it — and said: "You're allowed."

---

Evy pressed her hand to Marcus's arm, very gently, the way she did with objects — asking permission first with her eyes.

Marcus nodded.

Evy held on for a moment. Then she said: "Your mum used to call the hedgerow painting 'the one with the wrong rabbit.' Because you put a rabbit in it that was slightly too big."

Marcus made a sound that was not quite laughing and not quite crying and was quite a lot of both.

"She did," he said. "She really did."

Alex watched the mirror.

The grey fog around Marcus shifted. And then — slowly, slowly — the deep blue came back. And the warm ochre. And the green.

The buttercups in the mirror brightened.

All across the fifteen-acre field, without anyone being able to explain quite why, the afternoon light got about thirty percent warmer.

---

Marcus painted for another hour on the bridge. The painting was not perfect — the rabbit was too big again, which everyone agreed was correct — but the colours sang.

He thanked them six times. He gave Evy one of his spare pens. He told Amelia's dad that he had been very kind. He looked at Felix and Alex for a long moment.

"Which one of you noticed first?" he said.

"Alex," said Felix, immediately and loyally.

Marcus nodded. "Good noticing," he said to Alex. "That's a real skill. Don't stop."

Alex didn't say anything. He held the mirror up one last time. In it, Marcus was golden all the way through.

---

On the walk home, Amelia's dad and Evy's mum — who had arrived halfway through the second cup of tea and immediately understood the situation in the way that certain parents can — walked behind the children.

"Does this happen often?" said Evy's mum, whose name was also Alex, which everyone had decided to simply accept as the funniest possible situation.

"About twice a month," said Amelia's dad cheerfully. "I've stopped questioning it."

"Evy told me she found someone's whole Tuesday once," said Evy's mum. "In a hedge."

"Mm," said Amelia's dad. "That sounds right."

Ahead of them, Felix was doing an excellent impression of what it would look like if the wrong rabbit was actually the correct size and everyone else was wrong. Isla was laughing the gold laugh. Evy was turning the new pen over and over, reading its small history. Alex was still holding the mirror, watching the field.

Everything was every colour it was supposed to be.

---

*Postscript, filed in the Trio Force Case Notes, Wednesday afternoon:*

*Case: The Quiet Colour Drain — resolved.*
*Method: Noticing (A), Loudness (F), Listening (Isla, Evy), Tea (both Alexes, simultaneously, without coordinating, which is scientifically interesting).*
*Status of Marcus: Excellent. Has sent a postcard of the rabbit painting. The rabbit is enormous and correct.*
*Status of the quiet mirror: Retained for the evidence shelf. Handle with care. It also works on people who say they're fine.*
*Note from Alex (the boy): "I think things go quiet when nobody listens to them for a long time. Probably the same for people."*
*Note from Felix (the boy): "I am going to be loud about things for them until they remember."*
*Note from both Alexes (the adults): "Tea."*

---

*End of Case File*

---

Hope this one lands well — Alex gets the real heart of this one, the magic that suits him perfectly (seeing what's underneath), and the two parent Alexes felt like too good a coincidence not to lean into. The "wrong rabbit" moment is the one I'd watch for on the read-aloud. 🐇

*[END]*


===STORY===

# The Day Before Tomorrow

```meta
id: the-day-before-tomorrow
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.4
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 3 / enjoy 4 / audio 5 / craft 5
words: 1155
minutes: ~9
date: 2025-12-16
source_chat: Felix and Alex's adventure group
source_uuid: 8d93ba3d-2619-4e19-8e9a-8bace5d78b89
prompt: Alison, us another story about the friends where Felix and Alex are 9 and Theo and Violet aren't even born.
reviewed: yes (read/known)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
notes: DISCOVERY. Patience/anticipation theme. Confirm on read.
```

## Story

Felix and Alex were nine years old, and they both agreed that this was the most boring summer holiday they'd ever had. They'd read all their books, built every possible fort in their garden, and even organized their toy collection twice.

"There's nothing to do," Felix complained, flopping dramatically onto the grass under their apple tree.

"Nothing interesting ever happens here," Alex agreed, though he said it more quietly because he didn't like complaining as much as Felix did.

Their village felt too small and too quiet. The same people walked the same dogs down the same lanes every day, and nothing exciting ever seemed to happen to anyone.

"I wish we had some friends to play with," Felix said sadly. "Real friends who liked adventures and mysteries and interesting things."

"Mum says we might meet some when school starts again," Alex pointed out.

"That's weeks away," Felix groaned. "We'll die of boredom before then."

Just then, a moving van rumbled past their garden gate, followed by a small yellow car. Both vehicles turned into the driveway of the empty cottage two streets over - the one that had been for sale for months and months.

"New people!" Alex said, sitting up with interest.

"Maybe they have children," Felix added hopefully.

They scrambled to their feet and ran to the front gate to get a better look, but the moving van was too far away to see much detail.

---

"Should we go and introduce ourselves?" Alex wondered.

Felix was already heading for the house to ask permission. He was the kind of boy who acted first and thought about it later, while Alex was the kind who liked to plan things properly.

"Mum!" Felix called. "Can we go and welcome the new neighbors?"

Their mum appeared from the kitchen, wiping her hands on a tea towel. "That's very thoughtful of you. Perhaps you could take them some of those biscuits I made this morning?"

She packed a tin with homemade biscuits and gave them strict instructions about being polite and not staying too long if the new neighbors were busy unpacking.

"And," she added with a knowing smile, "try not to look too disappointed if they don't have children your age."

But as Felix and Alex walked down the lane toward the cottage, they were both secretly hoping very hard that the new family would have at least one child who might become a friend.

---

The cottage looked completely different with the moving van outside and boxes stacked on the front path. A woman with paint in her hair was directing the removal men, while a man tried to coax a very fat ginger cat out from under a bush.

"Excuse me," Alex said politely when the woman noticed them. "We live down the lane, and we brought you some welcome biscuits."

The woman's face lit up with a warm smile. "How lovely! I'm Mrs. Whitmore, and this is my husband, and that's our cat Wellington, who's decided he doesn't like moving house."

"We're Felix and Alex," Felix said, offering the tin of biscuits. "We live at number seven."

"How wonderful to meet some local children," Mrs. Whitmore said. "We've just moved from the city, and we were hoping to make friends quickly."

The twins' hearts sank a little. Mrs. and Mr. Whitmore seemed very nice, but there was no sign of any children.

"Are you settling in all right?" Alex asked politely.

"Oh, we're getting there," Mr. Whitmore said, finally managing to catch Wellington and tuck him under his arm. "Though I think some of our neighbors might be wondering what we're up to."

"What do you mean?" Felix asked, curious.

Mrs. Whitmore glanced at her husband, then smiled mysteriously. "Well, we've brought rather a lot of... unusual things with us. People might think we're a bit eccentric."

---

As if to prove her point, the removal men emerged from the van carrying what looked like an enormous puppet theater, followed by several large boxes marked "COSTUMES" and "MAGIC PROPS."

"Are you performers?" Alex asked, his eyes wide.

"We are indeed," Mr. Whitmore said proudly. "We run storytelling workshops for children. Puppet shows, magic tricks, treasure hunts - that sort of thing."

Felix felt his excitement building. "Real magic tricks?"

"Well, stage magic," Mrs. Whitmore laughed. "Though I suppose it feels quite real when you're watching it."

"And treasure hunts?" Alex added.

"Oh yes. We've organized treasure hunts in castles and forests and even underground caves. We're always looking for new locations and new ideas."

The twins looked at each other with growing excitement. Maybe the new neighbors didn't have children their age, but they had something even better - they were professional adventure-makers.

"Would you..." Felix began hesitantly, "would you ever want to explore our village? There might be good places for treasure hunts here."

"We'd love to show you around," Alex added eagerly.

Mrs. Whitmore's eyes sparkled. "What a lovely offer. We'd be delighted to have some local guides."

---

And that's how Felix and Alex spent the rest of their summer - not being bored, but helping Mr. and Mrs. Whitmore discover all the best secret places in their village.

They showed them the old mill wheel that still turned when the stream was high, the ancient church with its mysterious carved stones, and the hidden paths through Willow Woods. Mr. and Mrs. Whitmore listened to every story and made notes about which locations might work for future treasure hunts.

In return, they taught the twins some simple magic tricks, showed them how to make puppet voices, and even let them help plan a treasure hunt for the village children's party in August.

"You know," Mrs. Whitmore said one afternoon as they sat by the mill stream, "we're going to need local assistants for our workshops. Children who know the area well and can help other young people feel welcome."

"Really?" Felix said hopefully.

"Really. We think you two would be perfect. You're excellent guides, you're kind to newcomers, and you understand what makes a good adventure."

As they walked home that evening, Alex said thoughtfully, "I'm glad we met them, even though they don't have children."

"Me too," Felix agreed. "But you know what? I think when school starts and we meet new children, we'll be much better at making them feel welcome."

"Because the Whitmores made us feel welcome when we were the new ones meeting them," Alex realized.

"Exactly. And maybe someday, when new families move to the village, we can help make them feel at home too."

Looking back, Felix and Alex would always remember that summer as the time they learned that the best way to find good friends was to be good friends to others first - even if those others were grown-ups with puppet theaters and magic tricks instead of children their own age.

---

*The End*

*[END]*


===STORY===

# TRIO FORCE CASE FILE #32

```meta
id: trio-force-case-file-32
tier: A
status: keeper
score: 4.347
scores: affirm 5 / emotion 3.61 / enjoy 3.2 / audio 5.0 / craft 4.9
words: 1798
minutes: ~13
date: 2026-06-10
source_chat: Cloned heroes versus multipowered villain
source_uuid: 33e8d333-c2ce-49c4-a033-e8b4ac0d0237
prompt: a story with 4 Felix's (I think cloning might work as we don't know any other people called Felix, or maybe multiverse), Alex, Isla, Evi (Ev-ee). Felix wants there to be hundreds o
reviewed: auto-scored (confirm on read)
elevenlabs_status: not yet recorded
```

## Story

## **The Day of a Hundred Felixes**
### *(And Also Quite a Lot of Alexes)*

---

It started, as things often do, with Felix having an idea.

"Alex," said Felix, at breakfast, with the particular look he got when the idea was *very* big. "What if there were more of me?"

Alex looked up from his toast. "More of you."

"LOADS more of me. Like — twenty me's. All doing stuff at the same time."

Alex thought about this for exactly three seconds. "That's either the best idea you've ever had," he said, "or the most dangerous."

"BOTH," said Felix happily, and ate his toast in one enormous bite.

---

They were on their way to HQ — the garden shed that wasn't a shed — when Evy came running across the fifteen-acre field, her hair flying and her eyes wide.

"Something's happening in town," she said. "Something *weird.*"

"Weird good or weird bad?" said Felix.

"Weird *duplicating*," said Evy. "Mrs. Okafor's post office has got three Mrs. Okafors in it. Mr. Pemberton made seventeen identical loaves at the same time and they all came out of the oven *together*. And the fountain keeper says there are now four fountains."

Alex was already watching the sky. There was something in the air — a faint shimmer, like the world had been photocopied slightly wrong.

"HQ," said Alex.

---

Inside HQ, three gold question-mark boxes were waiting on the desks. Gold boxes. That meant today was significant.

Felix's box opened with a sound like a trumpet played by someone who'd never met a trumpet before — BWAAAAAAP — and out came a badge that said:

**MULTIPLIER — make up to twenty copies of yourself. They share your bravery. They do not share your sensibleness. Use wisely.**

Felix's face went *incandescent* with joy.

Alex's box opened quietly, as his always did, with a small chime like a single raindrop, and out came a small silver whistle with a note attached:

**RECALL — one blow brings every copy back. Two blows stops a multiplied villain. Three blows is for emergencies only. You'll know when.**

Evy's box had a small golden compass that didn't point north — it pointed toward *the original*. The real one. When things were copies, this would find the source.

"Right," said Felix. "TWENTY OF ME. LET'S GO."

"Wait," said Evy.

Felix had already gone.

---

Outside, Felix stood in the middle of the field, held up the MULTIPLIER badge, and pressed the little button on the back.

BWAAAP. BWAAAP. BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP BWAAAP —

Twenty Felixes stood in the field.

They all looked at each other.

Then they all said, at the same exact moment: "BRILLIANT."

Isla, who had come to find them, stopped at the gate and stared. "Oh," she said. "Oh *no.*"

"Oh YES," said twenty Felixes simultaneously.

Alex looked at all of them. Each one had Felix's exact expression — the big grin, the look that said *what can I jump off?* — and he felt a complicated mix of love and mild terror.

"**Remember,**" he said, loudly, "**you are all brave, and you are all kind, and you are all capable of great things.**"

Twenty Felixes nodded solemnly. Then one of them said "CAN WE FLY?" and they all started trying to fly at once.

---

But here was the problem.

In town, someone else was multiplying too.

His name was Professor Nought. And he was not happy.

Professor Nought had been a perfectly ordinary professor of nothing — that was his subject, Nothing, he'd written fourteen books about it — until one day he'd found a strange golden machine in his study that he didn't remember buying.

He'd pressed one button and given himself one extra power: the ability to copy himself.

That had been three weeks ago.

Now he had copied himself seven hundred times.

Also, accidentally, three of the powers he'd given himself had been: the ability to make excellent biscuits, extreme expertise at card tricks, and the ability to whistle very loudly. He wasn't sure these were useful supervillain powers. But he had them.

He also had: super speed, super strength, the ability to go invisible, the ability to fly, and the power to make anyone within ten metres sneeze exactly once.

The sneeze one was his favourite. He wasn't sure why.

He had arrived in town because he wanted more powers. He'd heard there was a team of children here who had a whole *shelf* of magical items.

He wanted that shelf.

He had seven hundred copies of himself.

He was — and this was the important thing — not evil, exactly. Just very bored of writing books about Nothing, and very excited about powers, and very bad at thinking through consequences.

When Alex's team arrived, Professor Nought was standing in the town square with seven hundred identical professors around him, all wearing the same slightly-too-large corduroy jacket, all adjusting the same wire-rimmed glasses, and one of them was doing an excellent card trick.

"Hello," said Professor Nought. All seven hundred of them said it. It was very loud.

"Hello," said Alex.

"HELLO!" said twenty Felixes. Also very loud.

Professor Nought looked at the twenty Felixes. Then he looked at Alex. Then at Evy and Isla.

"You're the ones with the shelf," he said.

"Yes," said Alex.

"I'd like the things on it please."

"No," said Felix. All twenty of them.

---

What followed was extremely chaotic and extremely fun, and later, no one could agree on exactly what order it happened in. What is known for certain:

🌀 Fourteen Felixes tried to build a wall out of themselves by standing on each other's shoulders. It worked briefly and then didn't.

🌀 Evy's compass spun wildly until she pointed it very carefully at one specific Professor Nought in the crowd — the original, third from the left, the one whose left shoe was untied.

🌀 Alex noticed that every time Professor Nought used a power, he looked slightly *sad* — like the power was fun for a second and then immediately not enough.

🌀 One of the Felixes sneezed (Professor Nought's sneeze power had caught him) and knocked over four other Felixes like dominoes, which made all twenty of them laugh for about forty-five seconds.

🌀 Isla very calmly walked up to one of the Professor Noughts, shook his hand, and said "Excuse me, which one are you?" and he said "I'm not sure any more," very quietly, and she said "I think that might be the problem."

🌀 Alex found the original Professor Nought — the one with the untied shoe — and stood very still in front of him, which was Alex's quietest and most powerful thing.

"Why do you want more powers?" Alex said.

Professor Nought opened his mouth. Closed it.

"I wrote fourteen books about Nothing," he said eventually. "And nobody read them."

"Oh," said Alex.

"I thought if I had enough powers — if there were enough of *me* — someone would notice."

There was a silence.

Then Felix — the *original* Felix — walked over. He looked at Professor Nought for a long moment.

"There's already enough of you," said Felix. "You just need better books. Or better friends. Probably both."

Professor Nought blinked.

"There's a support group," Felix added helpfully. "Thursdays. Janet makes shortbread. Victor's been working on a Victoria sponge."

"I do make good biscuits," Professor Nought said, very quietly.

"SEE?" said Felix. "BRING THOSE."

---

Alex blew the silver whistle once.

All twenty Felixes collapsed back into one Felix with a sound like a very satisfied FOOMP.

Then Alex blew it twice.

Seven hundred Professor Noughts rippled and folded and compressed until there was just one, slightly rumpled, with an untied shoe, standing in the town square with an expression of exhausted relief.

"That's better," said the professor. "I was getting confused about which memories were mine."

"Keep the biscuit power," said Felix generously. "You've earned it."

---

That evening, Daddy made tea.

He made one cup for each of them — Felix, Alex, Evy, and Isla — and set them on the kitchen table, and said nothing because he didn't need to, because the best parents always know when the right thing is just to put the tea down and be there.

"Daddy," said Felix, wrapping both hands around his cup. "Do you think I'm enough? Like. Just one of me?"

Robert looked at his son for a long moment.

"Felix," he said, "one of you is already more than most people can handle."

Felix grinned, enormous and satisfied.

Alex, next to him, said nothing — but his hand found Felix's under the table, and that said everything.

---

### 💫 YOUR AFFIRMATIONS FOR TODAY 💫

*From the files of Trio Force HQ — for reading aloud, at bedtime, or whenever you need them:*

⭐ **"You are enough, just as you are — one of you is already brilliant."**

⭐ **"Being brave means jumping in. Being wise means knowing where to land."**

⭐ **"The best teams need all kinds of people — the loud ones and the quiet ones."**

⭐ **"When you feel invisible, you don't need to multiply — you just need the right people to see you."**

⭐ **"You notice things. You do things. Together, you can do anything."**

*Recorded in the Trio Force logbook by Amelia (who arrived too late for the main event and is quietly furious about it):*

*Professor Nought attended the Thursday group the following week and brought lemon shortbread. Janet declared it "professionally threatening." This is the highest compliment Janet has ever given. He now attends every week and is working on a fifteenth book, this time about Something.*

*The Nought biscuit power has been deemed canon and will be useful in future. Evy has already written this down.*

*Felix attempted to multiply himself again at breakfast the next morning "just to try it." Mummy's expression was enough. He did not.*

*The original Alex, for the record, only had to blow the whistle once. He always only needs once.*
